style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"New Trends in Online Learning Small\" /></a><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472aa7200b-pi\" style=\"float: left;\" />Finished Netflix? Done with “doom-scrolling” social media? Maybe it’s time to gain those skills you have been putting off.</p>\r\n<p>The expansion of online learning was booming before COVID-19 emerged. Now, with the rise of work from home and homeschooling, the switch to online study has been massively accelerated.</p>\r\n<p>However, before enrolling in some uninspired port of traditional course content to an online platform, let's see what else is out there. What are the emerging trends and good practices? What can we look forward to seeing in the world of online learning for project managers?</p>\r\n<p><strong>Increased Focus on the Learner Experience</strong><br />Work-from-home orders aside, organizations typically struggle to get staff motivated to learn, whether for new skills acquisition or compliance training (safety, HR policies, etc.). At the same time, training platforms are competing to win market share by creating the most engaging frameworks and enjoyable learning experiences.</p>\r\n<p>The period from 2005 to 2010 brought YouTube, Twitter and iPhones. Searching for content and consuming videos would never be the same again. LMS (learning management systems) evolved to become LXP (learning experience platforms). These new platforms focus on content discovery, content recommendations, career paths, skills mapping and, in some cases, self-published content with automatic content indexing.</p>\r\n<p>We will look at some project management-based examples shortly, but first, let's examine how these systems differ from old training platforms. What do people want from a modern learning experience?</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Mobile-first:</strong> Content must be formatted to work on mobile devices such as phones and tablets, as well as larger computer screens. Research<sup>[1]</sup> from over 700 organizations indicates that employees typically only have 24 minutes a week for “formal learning.” Using a mobile device enables more learning opportunities.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Streamlined, reduced time to find content:</strong> Searching skills catalogs and competency frameworks is a drag. People want curated playlists, channels, and “top-rated”/“others enjoyed” smart content suggestions that we see on YouTube, Netflix and Amazon.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Personalized recommendations:</strong> Extending the easy-to-find concept, people want tailored recommendations based on their learning goals, career paths and current progress. These can be extended by AI-based suggestions and people's history of consumption.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Video-centric content:</strong> Organizations used to be worried about video-based content. Would people learn anything? Is just watching active enough? The popularity of YouTube how-to videos is living proof of the format. From wiring a socket to rebuilding an engine, videos provide a rich, high-bandwidth learning experience. </li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Micro-learning:</strong> Just enough, just in time. Our brains do not learn over long continuous periods; instead, we learn incrementally. Often, the best motivation for learning is having an immediate problem to solve. Microlearning uses short-form modules of four minutes or less, often with a video component, to answer an “I need help now” problem. It also drops a lot of the preamble and “why” background to focus on the “how.”</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Micro-credentialing:</strong> Online assessment allows for awarding micro-credentials. These are smaller achievements, such as electronic badges that recognize achieving minor learning goals. Frequent small rewards closely linked to recent performance is more motivating than less-frequent large rewards. Computerized tests and credentials are cheaper to administer and reward than physical ones so that they can occur more frequently. Many people are motivated by collecting badges and can display them on portals like ProjectManagement.com and LinkedIn.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Gamification:</strong> Micro-credentials tap into gamification, which is the neuroscience of rewards, motivation and psychology to encourage learning by making it more enjoyable. Other strategies include “keep the streak going” reminders, points, leaderboards and community features.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Interactive:</strong> Watching videos, listening to audio and reading text is a one-way flow of information with a decreasing information-retention rate over time. Productive learning environments punctuate this flow with interactive exercises to reset our focus and hit the “save” button on content. The best platforms mix in visual and text-based activities to break up content delivery, test understanding beyond regurgitation, and reset our focus.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Repetition and reinforcement: </strong>Unfortunately, our memory is weak, and retention fades over time. Studies on <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaced_learning\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">spaced learning</a> and skills acquisition show we need to review content multiple times and apply it in various settings to retain it. Language learning platforms such as DuoLingo does this well, requiring repetition and reinforcement in different contexts to ensure we master content.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>VR/AR:</strong> Some platforms use virtual reality and augmented reality to make training more relevant. For instance, oil rig workers can practice evacuation drills wearing headsets to show what they would see when navigating an emergency. Likewise, engineers can use augmented reality to identify aircraft parts and show torque settings and service recommendations using AR-equipped glasses.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p><strong>Project Management Examples</strong><br />Today, we can see instances of these new learning trends in products such as PMI's <a href=\"https://snippets.pmi.org/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Snippets</a> and the training elements of <a href=\"https://standardsplus.pmi.org/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">StandardsPlus</a>. These tools offer short-format, video-first, micro-learning options on project management topics. They are focused on explaining how-to content and incorporate some of the gamification and content curation features described.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Learning Cycles and Choices</strong><br />Micro-learning modules can seem fragmented to people used to full-length textbooks and traditional multi-day training courses. Like perpetually snacking instead of having proper meals, it may feel unorganized, trivial and too random. However, we need to remember that before COVID-19, employees often only experienced minimal periods for their on-the-job training.</p>\r\n<p>Micro-learning fits the time-pressured need, but there is still the market for longer macro-learning. Traditionally, this was at the beginning of careers or new roles and then supplemented by micro-learning while on the job (as pictured below):</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a31200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"Traditional Learning Cycle\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a31200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a31200b-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Traditional Learning Cycle\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>Now, with a work-from-home reset for many of us, maybe it’s an excellent time to insert some new opportunistic macro-learning as well as micro-learning (perhaps to learn about program management, Kanban or leadership—whatever you have hoped to achieve).</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a69200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"New Opportunity Learning Cycle\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a69200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a69200b-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"New Opportunity Learning Cycle\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>Macro-learning is the longer format, more focused training that often comes to mind when we think about learning a new skill. It includes multi-day courses and in-depth study with practice.</p>\r\n<p>However, this does not mean giving up on the learner experience trends discussed earlier. Options such as LinkedIn Learning uses many of these learning experience concepts and bundles micro-learning modules into more extensive courses and more substantial credentials.</p>\r\n<p>Other offerings in the macro-learning space include EdX, Coursera, Udemy, Udacity and NovoEd. Like most platforms, they contain some great content taught by experts—and some not-so-great content. However, with the option to read reviews, sort by top-rated courses and try free samples, much of the risk of choosing a poor curriculum can be avoided.</p>\r\n<p>So, if you have some time to gain new skills, do not settle for old LMS platforms with tired and uninspiring learning experiences. Lifelong learning should be fun and rewarding. Explore some of the latest offerings. Maybe the new formats, gamification or social aspects will be just what you need to stay focused and get more out of the process.</p>\r\n<p><strong><em>References</em></strong></p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://joshbersin.com/2018/06/a-new-paradigm-for-corporate-training-learning-in-the-flow-of-work/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Learning in the Flow of Work</a> by Josh Bersin</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>[Note: For more articles from Mike Griffiths, visit his blog at <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">www.LeadingAnswers.com</a>. Mike first wrote this article for ProjectManagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/622570/New-Trends-in-Online-Learning\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here]</a></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Mon, 11 May 2020 12:07:00 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e948122e200b-300wi","linkMd5":"ed38f6169dab723c1da42c07ea36b0e6","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn23@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-800_2fe1a6b50df7e24d.webp","destWidth":300,"destHeight":225,"sourceBytes":70399,"destBytes":9464,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e948122e200b-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn23@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-800_2fe1a6b50df7e24d.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a31200b-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn55@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-50-05-370_5cbd044c8317d0d7.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9472a69200b-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn52@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-50-05-513_ad66bba10c8381d9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Announcing Supported Study Groups","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2020/06/announcing-supported-study-groups.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20264e2e23d89200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"PMI-ACP Mike Griffiths\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20264e2e23d89200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20264e2e23d89200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"PMI-ACP Mike Griffiths\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>I am piloting a new program for anyone wanting to study for and obtain their PMI-ACP credential.</p>\r\n<p>It is a small group, 8-week online book-club / self-study program.</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li>Read one chapter of my PMI-ACP Exam Prep book each week</li>\r\n<li>Join me for a one-hour Zoom call to review topics and ask any questions you may have</li>\r\n<li>Get access to a private LinkedIn group where you can ask additional questions and discuss topics with peers</li>\r\n<li>Get exclusive chapter summaries, mindmaps, additional sample exam questions, and extra resources</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>At the end of the 8-week program, we will have covered all the material, and you should be ready to take the exam.</p>\r\n<p>This new public program is a pilot. I am currently running a similar program for a group of agile coaches, and feedback so far has been excellent. To make sure everyone has an opportunity to ask questions, I will cap each group at 20-25 people.</p>\r\n<p>I want to offer a more affordable option than online training for people who are willing to self-study. This option allows people to clarify topics with me and interact with others who are also preparing for their PMI-ACP exam.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Introductory price $99</strong>.</p>\r\n<p>This price includes the weekly Q&amp;A Zoom calls, LinkedIn support group, and exclusive resources (summaries, mindmaps, extra sample exam questions, etc)</p>\r\n<p>If you would like to participate or learn more, please send me an email:  </p>\r\n<p><strong>Mike &lt;at&gt; LeadingAnswers.com</strong></p>\r\n<p><strong>Logistics</strong>: I plan on running several groups concurrently with the weekly Zoom calls at different times, so we should be able to accommodate different time zones.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Fri, 12 Jun 2020 15:54:03 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20264e2e23d89200d-600wi","linkMd5":"c9c7b953fb9c8319debc8cd3050a9add","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn99@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-888_47a4486f292e4eaf.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":327,"sourceBytes":97888,"destBytes":20944,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20264e2e23d89200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn99@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-888_47a4486f292e4eaf.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"What’s in your backlog?","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/11/whats-in-your-backlog.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p>Let’s explore what you do and do not put in a backlog. How do these sound?</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li>Features and non-functional requirements – Absolutely</li>\r\n<li>Bug fixes and change requests – Yes, probably</li>\r\n<li>Risk avoidance and risk reduction activities – Sure, maybe</li>\r\n<li>Opportunity exploitation activities and marketing ideas – Now you’re just getting weird!</li>\r\n<li>Team building and social events – Erm, no!</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>Yet, if it’s all just stuff for the team to do, then why not put it in the backlog? Maybe because the customer has not asked for it and the product owner has to own and order it, but let’s look further.</p>\r\n<p>If we used a backlog metaphor for prioritizing backlog work items. It may look like this.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37d773d200c-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Backlog of Backlog Items\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37d773d200c image-full img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37d773d200c-800wi\" title=\"Backlog of Backlog Items\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>I am not suggesting these are the correct elements for including in a backlog, I am just showing the common ones. However, I am probably getting too abstract too quickly. Let’s start at the beginning.</p>\r\n<p><strong>A Backlog Primer</strong></p>\r\n<p>For agile teams, backlogs represent their To-Do list of work. All the things they need to complete before the product or project is done. Now, there may be interim releases. In fact, there should be interim releases delivering valuable functionality as soon as possible. However, there typically remains a list of remaining work. For long-lived products this list may never be emptied by the team, instead it is refreshed and reordered based on the latest priorities.</p>\r\n<p>While the team works from the backlog, it is typically prioritized by a product owner / business representative / ambassador user that sequences the work. This product owner manages the backlog, keeping things up to date with the latest product decisions. They also flesh-out items prior to work starting on them. Product Owners also answers questions about the work from the team, etc.</p>\r\n<p>Here’s a typical backlog showing a combination of features, change requests, bug fixes and a couple of risk reduction activities.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a3786d200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Backlog example\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a3786d200d image-full img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a3786d200d-800wi\" title=\"Backlog example\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Types not Granularity</strong></p>\r\n<p>This post discusses the types of things in a backlog, not the names we give different levels of granularity. Big chunks of work might be grouped into releases and then divided into themes, or features, epics, user stories and tasks as they get smaller and smaller. There is not an agreed to hierarchy at the large end of the spectrum, often teams miss out one or two of the theme / feature / epic options. However, most teams use the user stories and tasks as work gets smaller.</p>\r\n<p>Nevertheless, this post is about types of work, regardless of their size or what we call them.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Scrum Product Backlogs and Sprint Backlogs</strong></p>\r\n<p>Your view of a backlog may be different from mine. Most people I meet these days were introduced to backlogs through Scrum.</p>\r\n<p>The Scrum Guide describes the product backlog as an ordered list of everything that is known to be needed in the product. It is also the single source of requirements for any changes to be made to the product. The guide goes on to describe the sprint backlog as the set of product backlog items selected for the sprint, plus a plan for delivering the product Increment and realizing the sprint goal.</p>\r\n<p>My backlog history goes like this…</p>\r\n<p><strong>“It’s All Just Work We Have to Do”</strong></p>\r\n<p>I was first exposed to backlogs of work in the early 1990s. Working as a developer at Data Sciences Ltd in the UK I wrote a program to manage our work tasks on a government project. My project manager saw it one day and two interesting things happened.</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li>He did not chastise me for working on a side project of developing a visual work tracker rather than working on the client project.</li>\r\n<li>He asked why it did not contain all our bug fixes and change requests? I did not have a good answer, other than those are different buckets of work we should track separately. He dismissed this explanation and told me to add a flag if I wanted to track work types separately, and said “It’s all just work we have to do” and walked off, but his insight stuck with me. The class of work is secondary – all this stuff needs to get done.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>My visual work tracker was quite limited and I abandoned it. The database connection from Easel (a language better suited to building graphical UIs for mainframe systems) did not support concurrent users well. Yet, a couple of years later when we started creating DSDM I knew the backlog was “Just work we have to do”. The backlog is the input-hopper for team work. The product owner is the input-hopper custodian, often subject matter expert, and settler of priority and compromise disputes / negotiations.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong>Risks in the Backlog</strong></p>\r\n<p>I have been keen on proactively addressing risks for many years. Just as features deliver value, risks in the form of threats to the project cost money and cause delays - if they occur. As such, these threats are potentials for anti-value. Like bank deposits and bank-fees, the act of adding value and avoiding losses go hand-in-hand to maximize value.</p>\r\n<p>In the late 1990s I used RUP with some clients and was impressed by the Elaboration phase’s goal of tackling risks early in the project lifecycle. I corresponded with Philippe Kruchten, co-author of RUP, about how to illustrate the good work done on risk reduction during Elaboration that often did not have a lot to demo or show for it. I ended up creating Risk Burn Down graphs for my projects. I wrote about these ideas when I started blogging in 2006 as <a href=\"http://www.leadinganswers.com/2006/09/creating_risk_p.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Risk Profile Graphs</a>. By this time I’d been using them for 4-5 years and knew they were well received by sponsors and executives.</p>\r\n<p>Later in 2006, I wrote about <a href=\"http://www.leadinganswers.com/2006/11/smart_planning_.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">risk adjusted backlogs</a> and <a href=\"http://www.leadinganswers.com/2007/09/agile-risk-mana.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Agile Risk Management</a> explaining how to insert risk avoidance and risk reduction activities in the backlog. In 2012 I presented some <a href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/files/collabortive-games-for-agile-risk-management-1.pdf\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Collaborative Games for Risk Management</a> at the Agile 2012 Conference and PMI Global Conference. </p>\r\n<p>When members of the project management community read these posts and papers they correctly criticized my ignorance around proper risk management terminology. Risks, of course, can be positive (opportunities) or negative (threats). I was only talking about negative, potentially harmful risks (threats) when I talked about inserting risks based activities in the backlog. A real risk-adjusted backlog has both threat avoidance and reduction steps, as well as opportunity exploitation and opportunity enhancement actions.</p>\r\n<p>This is how we got to risk avoidance and opportunity exploitation activities in the backlog.  One aims to avoid costs, the other aims to generate new value. Risk management techniques like Expected Monetary Value converts probabilistic events into financial values. For instance, if we have a 50% risk of incurring a $400,000 loss then this event’s expected monetary value is 50% x $400,000 = $200,000.</p>\r\n<p>Likewise, we can assess opportunities too. If the average profit for new customers is $20,000 per annum, then we can determine if inviting qualified applicants on a factory tour with product demos and giveaways that costs us $500 per head is worth it at a 5% conversion rate. Here $20,000 x 0.05 = $1,000, so yes, it appears worth offering factory tours and giveaways for qualified leads.</p>\r\n<p>Multiplying guessed benefits or losses by guessed probabilities is an inexact science. However, it is one that the insurance industry has spent centuries trying to master. So they err in their favor and often price based on what the market will bear. Yet it happens throughout all forms of business and is the basis for taking an economic view of production that underpins all the return on investment and prioritization schemes such as Weighted Shortest Job First. We are constantly looking to maximize value.</p>\r\n<p>So, if a team building lunch is important for boosting performance or reducing the likelihood of conflict and delay, why not put it in the backlog? If it would be helpful for someone to walk the VP of sales through the latest product demo, put it in the backlog.</p>\r\n<p>The Product Owner remains the custodian of the backlog, but with some discussions around threats and opportunities, they often see the advantages of adding these other work types to the backlog. Taking an economic view of work allows us to decide “Where is the next best dollar spent’. That may well be on Feature X or a site visit to help build relationships and increase motivation.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p> </p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Tue, 27 Nov 2018 22:18:06 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37d773d200c-800wi","linkMd5":"24f73a668d7768a03fbb21850b6bd504","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn58@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-49-55-799_f5e6ff41392afcd0.webp","destWidth":684,"destHeight":648,"sourceBytes":72118,"destBytes":30106,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37d773d200c-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn58@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-49-55-799_f5e6ff41392afcd0.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a3786d200d-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn72@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-50-08-106_b33cd000ba500032.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"5 Major Changes Coming to the PMP Exam ","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/12/5-major-changes-coming-to-the-pmp-exam-.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2edfd200b-pi\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"5 Changes\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2edfd200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2edfd200b-300wi\" style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"5 Changes\" /></a>Some fundamental changes are coming to the PMP® exam. Currently slated for January 2021, the content and composition of the exam will be completely revamped. As described in the new <a href=\"https://www.pmi.org/-/media/pmi/documents/public/pdf/certifications/pmp-examination-content-outline-july-2020.pdf?v=d2061dbc-3a3a-4cf0-bb67-7e8e0429f8f8\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">PMP Exam Content Outline</a>, PMI commissioned a research study into trends in the project management profession. This study, called the Global Practice Analysis, investigated which job tasks and approaches people frequently use.</p>\r\n<p>The job task analysis identified the knowledge and skills required to function as a project management practitioner. Now the PMP is changing to better reflect these practices; here are some of the major changes:  </p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a51ac2200c-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"New Focus\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a51ac2200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a51ac2200c-75wi\" style=\"width: 75px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"New Focus\" /></a>1. New focus</strong>– Switching from the previous domains (initiating, planning, executing, etc.), the new exam will be based on three new domains: people, process and business environment. These new domains align more closely with the <a href=\"https://www.pmi.org/learning/training-development/talent-triangle\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">PMI Talent Triangle®</a>sections of leadership, technical project management, and business and strategic work.</p>\r\n<p>Since project management occurs in a variety of industries, the business environment domain only tests universal concepts and does not get into any specifics around project types. The split of questions between these domains is:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li>People: 42%</li>\r\n<li>Process: 50%</li>\r\n<li>Business Environment: 8%</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eeaa200b-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"New Content\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eeaa200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eeaa200b-75wi\" style=\"width: 75px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"New Content\" /></a>2. New content</strong>– The job task analysis revealed that many project managers are using agile approaches, or some agile concepts in hybrid life cycles. To reflect this, the new exam covers the complete value delivery spectrum including predictive, hybrid and agile approaches.</p>\r\n<p>The inclusion of agile concepts and increased emphasis on the people aspects of projects represent a big shift. Concepts like servant leadership, conflict resolution and retrospectives were previously the domain of the PMI-ACP® exam, but will now be featured more frequently on the PMP exam (although not in so much depth or frequency).</p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eed0200b-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"New Question Types\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eed0200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eed0200b-75wi\" style=\"width: 75px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"New Question Types\" /></a>3. New question types</strong>– A change announced by PMI at the recent PMI Global Conference in Philadelphia was the introduction of some new question types. PMI will be introducing question types that depart from the tradition multiple-choice format of four options and one correct answer.</p>\r\n<p>The new format questions include drag-and-drop and clicking on a graphic region. These new question types allow questions such as asking the test taker to select the graph/chart that best fits a described scenario, or identify what part of an image applies to a described situation.</p>\r\n<p>Crossword and coloring-in based questions will be added later (just kidding). Personally, I applaud the incorporation of visual questions; a large component of effective communication involves interpreting and creating graphs and charts, so any way to assess this capability is welcome.</p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eef8200b-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Move Away from PMBOK\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eef8200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eef8200b-75wi\" style=\"width: 75px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Move Away from PMBOK\" /></a>4. Moving away from the <em>PMBOK® Guide </em></strong>– The PMP exam is not a test of the <em>PMBOK Guide</em>.</p>\r\n<p>This concept is so fundamental—yet so universally misunderstood—that I feel the need to repeat it: The PMP exam is <em>not </em>a test about or on the <em>PMBOK Guide</em>. This misunderstanding may have arisen because the domains in the old PMP Exam Content Outline matched the process groups in the <em>PMBOK Guide</em>. This was a logical (but flawed) assumption.</p>\r\n<p>When question writers develop questions, they must reference at least two source documents for each question. This is to make sure the question is based on agreed-to sources and not just their belief or recommendation. Previously, the <em>PMBOK Guide</em> was frequently used as one of the sources, but it was always accompanied by at least one non-<em>PMBOK</em> source.</p>\r\n<p>Since the Global Practice Analysis and job task analysis identified more people-based skills and agile approaches, then increasingly, the sources referenced will not include the <em>PMBOK Guide</em>. By structuring the PMP Exam Content Outline around people, process and business domains, PMI is further signaling the departure from <em>PMBOK-</em>focused topics. The list of new source materials is available <a href=\"https://www.pmi.org/certifications/types/project-management-pmp/pmp-reference-list\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>.</p>\r\n<p>The takeaway for PMP aspirants is to base their studies on understanding and applying the concepts described in the domains, tasks, and enablers listed in the exam content outline.</p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ef22200b-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Education Evolution\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ef22200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ef22200b-75wi\" style=\"width: 75px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Education Evolution\" /></a>5. Education evolution</strong>– These radical changes were planned to be implemented in December 2019. However, perhaps in part to questions from the training community, the changes have now been deferred until July 2020.</p>\r\n<p>No doubt it will be a big change for Registered Education Providers (REPs) as they update their materials. Many PMP preparation courses followed the knowledge areas and domains of the old exam content outline. Now, with more of a focus on people and the decision to embrace the entire value delivery spectrum, training materials should be changed to better reflect the new exam content outline. This will take time but will result in a more practical exam.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Conclusion</strong><br />I welcome the change to make the exam more realistic and better aligned with how projects operate. The increased emphasis on the people aspects of projects more closely reflects where project managers spend the bulk of their time and attention. While the process groups and knowledge areas were useful buckets for organizing content, they did not really map how the project management activities integrate across multiple domains simultaneously.</p>\r\n<p>There will be an adjustment period as training companies adjust their materials. However, the end result will be an exam that better matches day-to-day work—which ultimately is where the exam should be moving to so that it’s a relevant assessment of project management activities.</p>\r\n<p>[This post first appeared without the list of source materials on projectmanagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/585909/5-Major-Changes-Coming-to-the-PMP--Exam\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>]</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Fri, 06 Dec 2019 13:07:00 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2edfd200b-300wi","linkMd5":"40570e71ed5f66723adc95990abf1b57","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn68@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-49-56-954_a80d363a87610946.webp","destWidth":300,"destHeight":181,"sourceBytes":47434,"destBytes":8372,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2edfd200b-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn68@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-49-56-954_a80d363a87610946.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a51ac2200c-75wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn4@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-50-08-700_73578cd7684cb191.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eeaa200b-75wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn67@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-50-08-048_ed77bdf2e8484794.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eed0200b-75wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn79@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-50-07-636_94a753788216af90.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2eef8200b-75wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn88@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-50-05-300_41c6c865a2be8f2b.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ef22200b-75wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn80@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-497_bf0632910e58e3fd.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Hybrid Knowledge: Expansion and Contraction","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/10/hybrid-knowledge-expansion-and-contraction.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8630200d-pi\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"Knowledge Expansion and Consolidation\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8630200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8630200d-300wi\" style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"Knowledge Expansion and Consolidation\" /></a>Expansion and Contraction</strong></p>\r\n<p>Project management requires the combination of technical skills, people skills and industry-specific knowledge. It is a true hybrid environment. This knowledge and its application also forms a beautiful paradox. Our quest to gain skills is never complete and always expanding, but the most effective tools are usually the simplest. Smart people do very simple things to achieve desired outcomes. Yet, they probably considered fifty alternatives before choosing the most effective, simple approach. You must know a lot to be confident your choice is apt.</p>\r\n<p>Knowledge and experience in project management follows the same pattern. Learning about project management, how to work effectively with people, and our industry domain is never complete. We then use this knowledge to choose the best action, which for ease of understanding and implementation, is usually a simple course of action. I call it Expansion and Contraction, but there is probably a simpler name I will learn about one day.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Learning as a Project Manager</strong></p>\r\n<p>One of the things I love about project management is the opportunity to expand our knowledge. There is so much to learn that is useful and applicable to projects. We also live in an age where there are more avenues for learning than ever before. Like a hungry kid in a candy store, the options seem endless and enticing.</p>\r\n<p>But what should we learn next to make the biggest impact? We could learn techniques to make us more effective or alert us to risks earlier. While earned-value is widely used, earned-scheduling is just getting started but promises useful tools. Alternatively, we will never be done learning how to better work with people. Communication, collaboration and motivation skills are more important than ever now talent is so mobile.  Likewise, expanding our industry and business skills are critical to build credibility with sponsors and useful collaborations with teams.</p>\r\n<p>The PMI Talent Triangle nicely describes these connected but infinitely extending fields of study.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37664b3200c-pi\"><img alt=\"Talent triangle tm\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37664b3200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37664b3200c-800wi\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Talent triangle tm\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>For learning purposes, the Strategic and Business Management segment includes all aspects of your industry. For example, if you work in IT, learning anything your team does or uses would be valuable.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Hybrid Learning Model</strong></p>\r\n<p>We should study topics from each of the Talent Triangle segments. However, it needs be fun to be sustainable. We learn best when we are interested and engaged, not when trying really hard to stay on topic or complete a task. Learning also needs to be balanced with other aspects of our lives. We need to look after ourselves and our relationships. We won’t perform or learn well if sick, depressed or lonely. (See the <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/01/project-you-and-project-two.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Project You</a> post for more on this idea.)</p>\r\n<p>When we get stuck, tired or burnt-out on one topic, switch to another after recording what is challenging. Our brains process things in the background. Often the simple act of recording that we are stuck on a topic yields an A-ha breakthrough days later in the shower or out on a walk. </p>\r\n<p>In addition to a stuck list, recognize all the things already studied. The following Kanban board has columns for To Learn, Studying Now, Stuck On, and Studied Already.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8591200d-pi\"><img alt=\"Learning Board\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8591200d image-full img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8591200d-800wi\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Learning Board\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>Personally, I try to limit my studying to one topic per Talent Triangle segment at a time. That’s my mental capacity, but I might mix in some short articles alongside a book on a similar topic.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Line Chefs not Eggheads</strong></p>\r\n<p>Knowledge is only useful if we can apply it when necessary. We want people who are humble and smart with a bias for action. When presented with a problem, recalling potential fixes is only half the solution. We then have to select one and try it otherwise we have analysis paralysis. The selection might be done individually or through discussions with the team, but we need to go from many options to a preferred one.</p>\r\n<p>Many people find having too many options with no clear preference overwhelming. Kicking around alternatives is good to select the best solution, but be aware of the anxiety this can cause. So keep it short. Power comes from agreeing and focussing effort on the selected approach. A 40 watt light bulb is barely enough to light a room. Yet a 40-watt laser beam will cut through cardboard and aluminum. It’s the same amount of light energy, just focussed in one direction.</p>\r\n<p>For me, there is an analogy or parallel between learning multiple skills and navigating. Once we know our way around we can create new pathways and connections. I live near the Canmore Nordic Centre. It has&gt; 100km of cross-country ski trails tightly winding through a heavily treed, mountainous park. It also has &gt; 100 km of summer mountain biking trails in the same space.</p>\r\n<p>People describe the trail network and map as confusing as a plate of spaghetti (summer trails) dumped on top of another plate of spaghetti (winter trails). It took me a couple of years of frequently getting lost to become comfortable navigating there. Now knowing both sets of trails allows me to create new loops by tagging trail segments together. It also allows me to get from point A to point B quickly or get back to the Day Lodge swiftly if needed. In short, learning where all the connections are allows us to link elements together for better flow and shortcuts.</p>\r\n<p>Learning as much as we can about project management, emotional intelligence and leadership builds similar skills. It allows us to see connections between ideas, link concepts together like creating a common vision for a project through storytelling.  Or, resolve conflict with empathy and appreciative inquiry.</p>\r\n<p>If we can layer these skills with learning more about our industry, then in the eyes of our sponsors, we go from effective employees to trusted advisors.</p>\r\n<p><strong>When We Get it Wrong</strong></p>\r\n<p>This is all great in theory, but we will inevitably screw-up sometimes. We will assess the options and gallantly blaze our way forward into bigger problems and unintended consequences. This is when being humble and flexible pay dividends.</p>\r\n<p>Just as a lack of direction in the face of uncertainty looks like fear or paralysis, then dogged adherence to a doomed plan looks like blind stupidity. By carefully framing decisions with qualifiers such as “Right now, our best course of action looks like X” or “We have decided to try Y for an iteration and evaluate the results” this way we reserve the right to be smarter tomorrow than we were yesterday.</p>\r\n<p>People are more likely to forgive a mistake and try another approach when it was originally positioned as today’s favoured strategy rather than our only hope. This is not to say we should get into the habit of failing and flip-flopping, just be smart enough not to get preachy about decisions in case the occasional one turns out to be a dud.</p>\r\n<p>So, strive for clarity with options to change direction if needed. We can explain: Here is what we are going to do... but, if along the way we learn of a better approach we reserve the right to revaluate and change direction. In fact, we have a duty to our sponsors to change direction if there ever looks like a better option.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Summary</strong></p>\r\n<p>If we cast the net wide and learn all that we can about project management, leadership and our industries we will never be bored or lacking topics to explore. The beauty comes when topics connect and we make links between subjects. Like always wondering where that unfamiliar road goes only to emerge from it one day and suddenly realize where you are and make the new mental connection.</p>\r\n<p>As we grow in our careers we see how management is really about leadership and leadership really starts with ourselves. Then a simple shift over here makes things go better over there. Project success is a hybrid of technical, leadership and strategic domains. As we grow we see more connections and then achieve more through doing less. It is great when it works but still uncomfortable when it fails so, follow the advice of Patrick Lencioni, and stay humble, hungry and smart.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[Note: I wrote this article for ProjectManagament.com first and it can be found <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/493977/Hybrid-Knowledge--Expansion-and-Contraction\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">here </a>- membership required ]</span></p>\r\n<p> </p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Mon, 29 Oct 2018 15:18:25 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8630200d-300wi","linkMd5":"31fcb374fbf23624193d8a18cbaf2d3a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn92@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-55-783_5dd6ee92f89d70c0.webp","destWidth":300,"destHeight":169,"sourceBytes":68100,"destBytes":10406,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8630200d-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn92@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-55-783_5dd6ee92f89d70c0.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37664b3200c-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn8@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-06-552_e73111323dab1e1f.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39c8591200d-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn31@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-461_dd365e93eeb93477.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Career Development in Overdrive","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/12/career-development-in-overdrive.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ab9630200c-pi\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"Overdrive\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ab9630200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ab9630200c-250wi\" style=\"width: 250px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"Overdrive\" /></a>In his best-selling book <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Drive-Surprising-Truth-About-Motivates/dp/1594484805/ref=sr_1_1?keywords=drive+dan+pink&amp;qid=1576902362&amp;sr=8-1\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Drive: The Surprising Truth of What Motivates Us</em></a>, Dan Pink explains three attributes (Autonomy, Mastery, and Purpose) that people need to feel satisfied and motivated at work. It is a great book, but we can do more. <em>Drive</em> only gets us started. As project managers, we can do more to help the people on the projects we manage.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Remember, Nobody Wants to Be Managed</strong><br />It’s important to remember that PMs manage projects, not the people working on the projects. Rarely can anyone effectively manage people, and rarer still are the people who want to be managed.</p>\r\n<p>Instead, <em>“We manage property and lead people; if we try to manage people\\e, they will feel like property.”</em> Over the long-term, people are more satisfied when they have control or input into how they undertake their work. Using our own skills, insights and decision-making process is more rewarding than blindly following instructions.</p>\r\n<p>Yet there are a few instances when we want to be told what to do. These include emergency situations or a scenario that is totally foreign—such as a temporary volunteer role. However, they are not common, and satisfaction at work requires freedom of choice in <em>how</em> to work—and an opportunity to bring our skills to bear on a problem.</p>\r\n<p>This freedom and skill opportunity make up the “autonomy” and “mastery” components of Dan Pink’s motivation triad (which also includes purpose). Pink explains what people want to feel satisfied and fulfilled. Here’s the recap…</p>\r\n<p><strong>What People </strong><em><strong>Do</strong></em><strong> Want Instead</strong><br />When we can provide freedom of choice (autonomy) with an ability to apply and learn new skills (mastery) in an environment that pursues a worthwhile goal (purpose), the magic happens. Now people are motivated. Then our role as leaders and managers of projects is to channel and coordinate these driven contributors in their pursuit of that goal.</p>\r\n<p>If that sounds unrealistically optimistic, maybe you have never felt or seen true motivation. Maybe YourGreedyCorp’s reason for being (max profits for owner and shareholders) lacks any sense of purpose for its employees?</p>\r\n<p>Unfortunately, organizations with uniting and worthwhile missions are scarce. We cannot all work for companies like <a href=\"https://www.patagonia.ca/company-info.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Patagonia</a>, <a href=\"https://www.tesla.com/en_CA/about\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Tesla</a> or <a href=\"https://www.buurtzorg.com/about-us/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Buurtzorg</a>, so how can we support people working in more traditional organizations?</p>\r\n<p>If we cannot influence the purpose of our organization, we can try to find the compelling purpose for our project and create as much autonomy and opportunities for mastery as possible. Then we can see what other motivational factors people desire.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Beyond “Drive” Enablers – Into “Overdrive”</strong><br />A recent <a href=\"https://www.entrepreneur.com/article/337055\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Entrepreneur article</a> about seven basic human needs provides great insights into a holistic set of motivational/satisfaction ideas we can bring to teams. Let’s review them:</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><em><strong> Safety/security: - </strong></em><strong>“<em>Without it, we feel anxious.” </em></strong>Creating a psychologically safe environment is critical for experimentation and learning. People need to feel it is not only okay for them to extend themselves and try new things, but are positively encouraged to do so.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">As leaders, we can help by modeling the desired behavior and talking about our own learnings and failures. For example, “<em>I made a mistake on the status report I am sending it again</em>” or “<em>I heard my presentation to the steering committee was too technical; I would like your help explaining it in simpler terms.</em>”</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">This second example also demonstrates vulnerability by asking for help. It turns out vulnerability in leaders is less a sign of weakness and more a bridge to building stronger relations with team members. Creating a safe environment and being human produces a better working environment.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">People join companies because of the opportunities and leave them because of the managers they encounter there. Don’t be one of those managers.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"> </p>\r\n<ol start=\"2\">\r\n<li><em><strong> Variability/challenge: - </strong></em><strong>“<em>Without it, we are bored.” </em></strong>Our brains are wired to seek new experiences and solve problems. It releases dopamine, a neurotransmitter that activates the pleasure circuits in the brain. It is likely to reward exploration and learning, which is critical for our evolution.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">We can take advantage of the motivation circuits everyone comes wired with by presenting work as problems, not tasks. Outline the problem, let the team tell you how it intends to solve it. This will usually be how you would have structured work tasks, but it might be a better plan. Then, providing it is legal and appropriate, let the team execute its plan. Now the team members have ownership of the approach and are much more invested in ensuring its success.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">It’s not rocket science, it’s neuroscience—and very well worth the extra steps of framing new work as problems and vetting proposed solutions.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"> </p>\r\n<ol start=\"3\">\r\n<li><em><strong> Status/significance: - </strong></em><strong><em>“Without it, we feel undervalued.” </em></strong>We need to recognize the role and significance people play on projects—and not just at the end of them. That is too little, too late. Instead, we need to find ways to acknowledge and explain the contributions people are making on a regular (say weekly) basis.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">It does not have to be much, but it does have to be <em>something</em>. People working without recognition can slide into doing the minimum tolerated. Instead, people who know their work is recognized will be more likely to try and do it well.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">Yes, making sure we talk to everyone about their work each week takes time and effort. However, it is much easier than explaining lackluster performance to executives or recruiting new team members because someone quit or was fired for underperforming.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">People need momentum to push past the obstacles they will inevitably face on projects. Regular recognition of their work creates and builds that momentum.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"> </p>\r\n<ol start=\"4\">\r\n<li><em><strong> Love/connection: - </strong></em><strong><em>“Without it, we are lonely.” </em></strong>We spend a large proportion of our waking hours at work. As social creatures, we crave some connection—but the depth varies from person to person. For some, it is enough to be recognized and have your name remembered. For others, they want to learn about their teammates and share their interests and dreams.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">HR departments can get justifiably concerned about inappropriate friendliness, and so often tend to err on the transactional side of interactions—e.g., <em>“Bill, here is the marketing report you asked for.”</em> However, this misses the opportunity to connect. “<em>Hey Bill, there you are…here’s that marketing report. How did Wendy do at her first basketball game?</em>”</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">Having someone that cares enough to ask how you are—and also learn whatever aspects you want to share—is significant. We should try to learn what our team members like to do in their spare time, what causes and activities they enjoy.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">People make terrible interchangeable cogs in a machine. They are inconsistent, unreliable and malleable, always changing shape. However, they make fantastic flowers in a garden. Some like shade, some like the sun; once we learn more about them, we can help them flourish.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"> </p>\r\n<ol start=\"5\">\r\n<li><em><strong> Self-expression: - </strong></em><strong><em>“Without it, we can’t express our personality.” </em></strong>We need to let people show us who they are. Let them customize their work environment appropriately. If working the front desk of a fancy hotel, that might not be much visually; but in other environments, it could be considerable.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">Dress, music, work time flexibility to go rock climbing or see the new <em>Star Wars</em> movie all show we recognize what is important to people. We hire people for who they are and their ability to contribute. Let’s not forget <em>who</em> they are, or they will forget to contribute.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"> </p>\r\n<ol start=\"6\">\r\n<li><em><strong> Growth/knowledge: - </strong></em><strong><em>“Without it, we feel stuck.” </em></strong>This is the “mastery” component of Dan Pink’s three motivators. People want to improve their skills. They want to get better at the craft they chose as a career. Providing training, access to education and conferences improves job performance, employee satisfaction, and retention.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">Education and training not only improve people’s sense of job worth, but it also improves their <em>self-worth</em>. People recognize and remember that.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"> </p>\r\n<ol start=\"7\">\r\n<li><em><strong> Contribution: - </strong></em><em><strong>“Without it, we don’t have the satisfaction of helping someone.”</strong> </em>This is the “purpose” component from Pink’s <em>Drive </em>book. People seek meaning, a purpose, an opportunity to help build a valuable legacy.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">Connect work to a worthy goal. People do not want to spend a day pushing keyboard buttons. They want to know they helped people get access to healthcare or education, or book a fun night out with friends.</p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"> </p>\r\n<p><strong>Putting the Seven Human Needs to Work</strong><br />Great leaders and the best project managers do all of these things instinctively. They weave and blend the concepts into every interaction and conversation they have each day. The rest of us likely need some coaching until we feel natural—and then they become habits to us.</p>\r\n<p>There is nothing wrong with creating a checklist of people to go talk with each day. We do not want to use the same questions, but instead develop interest, appreciation, and opportunities for growth.</p>\r\n<p>Demonstrate the desired behavior, admit your failings and concerns. Ask questions, be upbeat, seek connections and knowledge. Everyone in a leadership role is being watched and judged, whether they like it or not. So, behave as if every action—seen or unseen—is being scored. Integrity and transparency remove the need for remembering the story for this situation, or what you told who.</p>\r\n<p>When outlining work, start and end with the problem. Let people decide how best to solve it, and invite their ideas. Providing the solutions are legal and efficient, let them run with them. Check-in frequently to see how things are going. Investigate failures calmly. What can we learn? What can we do differently next time?</p>\r\n<p>It takes time, but it gets easier. Then people start helping you when they see you are not a total jerk. Then things get much easier, and it is tempting to think you are hot stuff and become a jerk. Stay humble, stay thankful and always be learning.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Summary</strong><br />Dan Pink’s <em>Drive</em> motivators of autonomy, mastery, and purpose are critical in ensuring motivation at work. However, they are a subset of the seven universal human needs we all seek to feel satisfied.</p>\r\n<p>As we manage our projects and lead our teams, we should check to see how many of them we can engage every day.</p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[An earlier version of this article first appeared on ProjectManagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/574834/Career-Development-in-Overdrive\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>]</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 11pt;\">(See <a href=\"http://www.LeadingAnswers.com\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">www.LeadingAnswers.com</a> for the full list of articles from Mike Griffiths)</span></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Sat, 21 Dec 2019 04:44:10 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ab9630200c-250wi","linkMd5":"092dafc92a6e84e2ed1dbaf113dc98b2","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn4@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-55-802_de313a79f6a544b2.webp","destWidth":250,"destHeight":213,"sourceBytes":32824,"destBytes":6462,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ab9630200c-250wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn4@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-55-802_de313a79f6a544b2.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Incubating Innovation","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/06/incubating-innovation.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ed81200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Innovation\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ed81200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ed81200d-300wi\" style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Innovation\" /></a>If success goes to those who can innovate the fastest, how do we nurture innovation? The basics are simple to understand—but difficult to implement and stick with in the face of adversity. We need to create an environment that encourages experimentation while also tolerating, investigating and learning from the inevitable failures.</p>\r\n<p>It may sound easy, but executives and shareholders demand results, not “learning opportunities.” We need an approach that fosters experimentation and learning in a defendable way, with a bias for results. To innovate faster than our competitors, we need to maximize our learning potential. This means that by design, 50% of our experiments should fail since we are seeking knowledge expansion, not validation of things we already know. The trick is keeping people engaged and motivated when half of their experiment time is spent failing.</p>\r\n<p>It starts with leadership and cascades down to a shared mindset of team members. Leaders need to be transparent about their own mistakes and learning moments. They need to model the desired behavior, share what they have learned and their new plan of action.</p>\r\n<p>These can be strategic learnings (“<em>Our European market testing has been poor, we are reworking the price options</em>”) or personal (“<em>Feedback on my presentation to investors indicated I was too technical; I need to find simpler ways to describe our technology”). </em>Until team members see transparency in common use, they will be reluctant to practice it themselves for fear of reprisal or criticism.</p>\r\n<p><strong>We Are in the Maze-Solving Business</strong></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ede7200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Maze 1\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ede7200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ede7200d-800wi\" title=\"Maze 1\" /></a><br />Developing new products or services is a maze-solving exercise. Nowadays, it is also a race. We need to find a workable solution faster than our competitors. There will be obstacles and dead-ends along the way, and that is okay. We must not let them demoralize us; we just need to learn from them, not repeat them, and keep going.</p>\r\n<p>The process of learning starts with understanding the knowable and then adding to this through experiments and new learnings. So, we start with smart people who understand their industry domain, technology and the business goals. We then need to create an environment with a dual track of product development and experimental learning.</p>\r\n<p>Organizations that run more experiments and iterate faster also learn faster. Scientists studying inheritance use mayflies because they reproduce and provide experimentation results so quickly. The more experiments you can run in a year, the more you can learn.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Our Mazes are Really Big</strong></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490edfd200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Maze 2\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a490edfd200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490edfd200d-800wi\" title=\"Maze 2\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>Developing a new product does not happen overnight. Outside of movies, rarely do lone geniuses develop a marketable product themselves. Instead, it takes teams of subject matter experts months to create proof-of-concepts and multiple iterations of tweaks to complete a viable product. These teams need support and coordination services throughout the process.</p>\r\n<p>Sponsors, executives and shareholders need plans, projections and updates. Product managers, project managers and team leaders all play an important role in keeping the maze-solving teams motivated and moving in the right direction. They also need to keep funding and support going while providing inputs about changing market demands and conditions.</p>\r\n<p>It sounds a difficult balancing act, but approaches such as design thinking, lean startup and agile provide stewardship models for development with inbuilt experimentation, observation and learning. What gets less attention is motivating teams to persevere despite the many failures encountered when experimenting for learning, not just validation.</p>\r\n<p><strong>The “Success Leads to Happiness” Fallacy</strong><br />Most people start their lives with the mistaken view that success leads to happiness. Our internal dialog creates a series of “if/then” scenarios:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li>“<em>IF I pass this exam, THEN I will be happy.”</em></li>\r\n<li><em>“IF I get this job, THEN I will be happy.”</em></li>\r\n<li><em>“IF we finish this project on schedule, THEN I will be happy.”</em></li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>However, the brain has a knack of moving the goalposts. We might be happy briefly, but then we quickly focus on the next exam, an even better job or a more ambitious project.</p>\r\n<p>While it is good to progress in life, we should not connect achieving a goal with achieving happiness. Instead, we need to understand that happiness is only 10% extrinsic (external things that happen to us, like success) and 90% intrinsic (how we think and feel about things).</p>\r\n<p><strong>A Happy Brain is a Productive Brain</strong></p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b59576200b-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Dopamine\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b59576200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b59576200b-800wi\" title=\"Dopamine\" /></a><br /></strong></p>\r\n<p>Happy workers are more productive and creative than stressed or unhappy workers. In our brains, the chemical dopamine is the neurotransmitter responsible for sending reward-motivated happiness signals. Put more simply, dopamine is a happiness chemical—it gets released when we are happy. Interestingly, dopamine also switches on more learning circuits in our brains.</p>\r\n<p>Research <sup>[1,2,3]</sup> shows that happiness improves work performance. Happy people are 31% more productive, happy doctors are 19% more accurate at diagnosing correctly, and happy salespeople are 37% better at sales.</p>\r\n<p>This intuitively makes sense. When we are unhappy or stressed, the brain prioritizes circuits for survival. If you spot a sabre-toothed tiger, it is probably best to focus on escape rather than contemplating the interplay of sunlight and shadows through the leaf canopy. Yet when searching for an innovative solution, we want all these extra brain circuits engaged. This brings us full circle on the <em>“I’ll be happy when I am successful”</em> logic. It turns out, being happy actually activates more of the brain to help us be successful.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ee22200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Success Fallacy\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ee22200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a490ee22200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"Success Fallacy\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Nurturing Happy Teams</strong><br />So, if the smartest workers are happy workers, how do we make them happy? Well, we don’t “make them happy” at all—that would be trying to force it in externally, using weak extrinsic motivators. Instead, we equip them with the tools to help build intrinsic happiness themselves.</p>\r\n<p>This might be sounding more touchy-feely than you are comfortable with. However, hard economics show that happy workers also persevere with problems longer, take less sick days, quit less and sue for wrongful dismissal much less, too…so let’s suspend the cynicism for a moment.</p>\r\n<p>The good news is that with as little as 30 minutes a day, measurable increases in dopamine levels are achieved in three weeks. So, if we can encourage these behaviors and turn them into habits, we get happier, healthier, smarter and more productive workers.</p>\r\n<p>These 30-minute exercises don’t even require expensive equipment or management consultants. They are simple, backed by research and include:</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><strong>3 Gratitudes</strong> – a daily recording of three new things you are grateful for <sup>[4]</sup></li>\r\n<li><strong>Journaling</strong> – recording positive experiences from the past 24 hours <sup>[5]</sup></li>\r\n<li><strong>Exercise</strong> – increases blood flow to the brain and helps eliminate toxins <sup>[6]</sup></li>\r\n<li><strong>Meditation</strong> – resets multi-tasking fatigue and helps with concentration <sup>[7]</sup></li>\r\n<li><strong>Acts of Kindness</strong> - helping others and saying “thanks,” which makes us feel better <sup>[8]</sup></li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>Organizations spend vast sums of money hiring smart people and providing them with complex tools and training. In comparison, the cost/benefit potential in investing and encouraging team happiness is extremely compelling. As project managers, team leads and executives, we need to be conscious of the behaviors we model, because people are watching us.</p>\r\n<p>If not already doing so, we can use these techniques ourselves to improve our own happiness and productivity—then share and encourage others to make use of them. A great benefit of intrinsic motivators is that they can be applied anywhere. It does not matter if you work in a toxic environment or report to a jerk. There is likely nothing stopping you making sure the first email of the day you send is to thank someone for their help or contribution. Also, no one will know if you make notes about positive experiences.</p>\r\n<p>People with budgets and hiring authority do know that yoga instructors are much cheaper than labor relations consultants and HR lawyers. Longer lunch breaks and fun team activities may require some explanations, but improved problem solving, fewer sick days and better ideas are definitely worth it.</p>\r\n<p>Don’t wait until project completion to celebrate team achievements. That’s too little, too late—and our brains have moved the goalposts to be thinking of the next project. Instead, celebrate the little things that recognize effort, persistence and displaying a good attitude.</p>\r\n<p>Obviously, it is not as simple as “happy people are the perfect innovators.” There are some concerns that people characterized as “happy” might be less likely to spot certain types of risks. Optimism needs to be tempered with realism. However, given the variable success rates of helping people become happier (“leading horses to water,” etc.), there will still be some pessimists; but on the whole, it creates a positive change that is worth the risk.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Summary</strong><br />Smart companies know the future belongs to the best innovators. Building and maintaining teams of productive innovators requires investment in tools, techniques and people. We need to have the right tools and be using today’s techniques such as the design thinking, lean startup and agile approaches. Then it comes down to our people. An appreciation of what truly makes us happy—and its effects on success—is a great starting point.</p>\r\n<p>Most organizations are not R&amp;D labs, so we need to balance innovation with everyday production and service. The mindset and changes described here may feel uncomfortable (or even unprofessional) at first. However, a quote from Eric Shinseki explains that <em>“If you dislike change, you're going to dislike irrelevance even more.”</em> It reminds us that the business landscape is changing faster than ever—and that we need to change with it to stay valuable.</p>\r\n<p><strong><em>References</em></strong></p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.laughteronlineuniversity.com/the-happiness-advantage/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">The Happiness Advantage</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.researchgate.net/publication/7419311_The_Benefits_of_Frequent_Positive_Affect_Does_Hapiness_Lead_to_Sucess\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">The Benefits of Frequent Positive Affect: Does Happiness Lead to Success?</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://wrap.warwick.ac.uk/63228/7/WRAP_Oswald_681096.pdf\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Happiness and Productivity</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://greatergood.berkeley.edu/images/application_uploads/Emmons-CountingBlessings.pdf\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Counting Blessings Versus Burdens</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.researchgate.net/publication/6873632_How_Do_I_Love_Thee_Let_Me_Count_the_Words_The_Social_Effects_of_Expressive_Writing\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">How Do I Love Thee? Let Me Count the Words, The Social Effects of Expressive Writing</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3137947/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Behavior Matters</a></li>\r\n<li><a rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">One Second Ahead</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"http://sonjalyubomirsky.com/wp-content/themes/sonjalyubomirsky/papers/LDinpressb.pdf\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Interventions to Boost Happiness and Buttress Resilience</a></li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[Note: I first wrote this article for ProjectManagement.com <a This makes it the most popular experience based agile certification and the credential of choice for hiring managers looking for the rigor of a ISO 17024 backed PMI credential. </p>\r\n<p>On March 26, 2018 the PMI updated the exam to align it with the lexicon of terms used in the new <a href=\"https://www.pmi.org/pmbok-guide-standards/practice-guides/agile\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Agile Practice Guide</a>. The course features updated materials and the new Updated Second Edition of my PMI-ACP Exam Prep book as an accompanying textbook.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong>My Involvement in the PMI-ACP Credential</strong></p>\r\n<p>I was a founding member of the steering committee that designed and developed the exam content outline for the exam. We based the exam on what agile practitioners with a year or two’s experience should know to be effective. We wanted a methodology agnostic credential that captured the agile practices used on most projects most of the time. The exam covers Lean, Kanban and agile approaches such as Scrum and XP along with servant leadership and collaboration. </p>\r\n<p>I worked with RMC to write their best-selling PMI-ACP Exam Preparation book. I recently updated this book to align it with the March 26, 2018 lexicon harmonization and change the chapter review questions to situational questions. The book is available from RMC <a href=\"http://store.rmcproject.com/pmi-acp-exam-prep-second-edition?_ga=1.151898225.918430629.1441829040\">here</a> and is also included in the course.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong>Details about the Course</strong></p>\r\n<p>The course will be capped to 20 people for better Q&amp;A and will likely take place at historic Fort Calgary which is close to downtown on 9<sup>th</sup> Avenue, has great catering and free parking.
