@LeadingAnswers.com.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Thu, 29 Mar 2018 23:17:04 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e201bb09ff9b63970d-250wi","linkMd5":"46915a94b5080ae287039d633c2e2a37","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn51@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-56-089_f63bf64854dc33a2.webp","destWidth":250,"destHeight":324,"sourceBytes":89870,"destBytes":18208,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e201bb09ff9b63970d-250wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn51@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-56-089_f63bf64854dc33a2.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995275},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Returning to the (Electronic) Cottage","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2020/07/returning-to-the-electronic-cottage.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9542d5e200b-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Electronic Cottage\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20263e9542d5e200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9542d5e200b-300wi\" style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Electronic Cottage\" /></a>This is not a post about rich people now able to visit their second homes after the lockdown, instead, a revisit of the concepts of decentralized work being the new way of undertaking projects.</p>\r\n<p>In 1980, Alvin Toffler’s book <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Third-Wave-Alvin-Toffler/dp/0553246984/ref=sr_1_2?dchild=1&amp;keywords=the+third+wave&amp;qid=1589990196&amp;sr=8-2\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"><em>The Third Wave</em></a> introduced the idea of “<strong>The Electronic Cottage</strong>” as the modern workplace where information technology allows more people to work from home or wherever they want. Toffler was a futurist and businessman who did not get the attention he deserved. Even though Accenture identified him as one of the most influential voices in business leaders (along with Bill Gates and Peter Drucker), we do not hear much about him.</p>\r\n<p>When I was at university in the 1980s, we were required to read <em>The Third Wave</em>. At the time, I was more interested in learning about compiler design and database structures, but I read the book and the ideas stuck. Thinking back, The Third Wave, along with Zen and Art of Motorcycle Maintenace, are the only books from my entire degree that I still remember.</p>\r\n<p><strong>The First and Second Waves</strong><br />The first wave was the agricultural revolution when hunter-gathers started farming and settled in villages.</p>\r\n<p>The second wave was the industrial revolution, when cheap, non-renewable fossil fuel energy was used to leapfrog previous levels of productivity. This industrialization required mobility from the workforce, and people moved from villages into cities to work in mills and factories. This movement resulted in the end of the large multigenerational families rooted to the soil.</p>\r\n<p>The “nuclear family” (of father, mother and a few children, with no burdening relatives) became the standard, socially approved model for industrial societies. Schools started emphasizing punctuality and rule-following to condition children for working in factories.</p>\r\n<p><strong>The Third Wave</strong><br />The “third wave” is the information revolution. It is what Peter Drucker called the knowledge worker age. What set Toffler apart was his ability to see how the second industrial age must end and why the information age was inevitable in 1980, more than 10 years before the internet was invented (let alone became popular).</p>\r\n<p>Toffler described factors that make the continuation of the second wave impossible, including: “The biosphere will no longer tolerate the industrial assault” and “Non-renewable energy sources are drying up (one of the hidden subsidies of the Second Wave).”</p>\r\n<p>He went on to describe factors that made the third wave possible and inevitable. These included:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Cheaper electronics and computers:</strong> “If the auto industry had done what the computer industry has done in the last 30 years, a Rolls-Royce would cost $2.50 and get 2,000,000 miles to the gallon.” Computing is cheaper and more powerful than ever.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>De-massification of the media:</strong> As the quantity of information available to people expands, they become less and less able to cope with it all. People fall back to paying attention to only what is important to them. We see a rise in the number of specialty channels appealing to narrow segments of the population.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>An intelligent environment:</strong> Home computers networked together and notifying us of weather alerts, home security alarms, etc. What we now call the connected home and IoT.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>A new social memory:</strong> Originally, human groups stored their shared memories in the minds of individuals (tribal elders, wise men, etc.). The second wave moved beyond memory by spreading mass literacy. Libraries and museums were built. By increasing the store of cumulative knowledge, it accelerated all the processes of innovation and social change. Now information is stored electronically and can be readily searched by all.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>The fact these predictions came true show the credibility of Toffler’s forecasts for the electronic cottage. Our recent work-from-home mandate has accelerated the transition to the electronic cottage, and maybe some of Toffler’s other predictions about changes to work and society will come true also?</p>\r\n<p>Here are a few things he said about this shift in working practices. The opening points are ideas from the book; the thoughts that follow are observations from today…</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><strong> IT makes it possible to work from home.</strong>Computers and electronic communications make it possible for many types of work to be done from home.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p><em>Observation from today:</em> Recent work-from-home mandates have pushed even laggards of this technology to try it and work through the kinks. While everyone wishes for freedom to return, maybe we can capitalize on the positive aspects of working from home, or from a favorite café, or a pleasant, local co-working space?</p>\r\n<ol start=\"2\">\r\n<li><strong> Commuting diminishes.</strong>Consider the cost incentives to companies. Commuting, which they indirectly subsidize, runs an average of 29 times as much as the installation of telecommunication equipment in a person’s home. Also, considerable savings in real estate costs, capital building investments, and building maintenance can be had. Staying at home will also reduce pollution and the cost of cleaning it up.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p><em>Observation from today:</em> Most organizations do not need to pay for any additional equipment since many people have high-speed internet and their own computers. People may currently have less-than-ideal working conditions with children being home from school, but once they return, do you want to go back to commuting with the associated cost and time drains?</p>\r\n<ol start=\"3\">\r\n<li><strong> Shorter work week:</strong>On the home side, as shorter workweeks become common, the higher ratio of commuting time to working time becomes more irrational, frustrating and absurd. Millions of jobs could shift out of the factories and office into which the second wave swept them and right back where they came from originally: the home. If this were to happen, every institution we know—from the family to the school and the corporation—would be transformed.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p><em>Observation from today:</em> Our current glimpse of homework and closer family relationships is artificial and lacking many of the benefits of being able to escape to the company of friends when we want to. The FIRE movement (Financial Independence, Retire Early) has already seen people reduce the number of hours they work, consume less and spend less on nonessential items like expensive commuting options. Few people have been talking about impacts on family, but the nuclear family might become a relic of the past.</p>\r\n<ol start=\"4\">\r\n<li><strong> Customization of products and in-home production:</strong>Most highly developed countries will concentrate on the creation of one-off and short-run manufactured goods depending on highly skilled labor and automated production systems. Customization will lead to the manufacture of one-of-a-kind products with items custom-made for individual users. This home-centered society will bring many changes:</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li>Greater community stability due to less forced mobility, less stress on the individual, fewer transient human relationships, and a greater participation in community life.</li>\r\n<li>Energy requirements will be reduced due to energy decentralization. Energy demand would be spread out, making it easier to use solar, wind and other alternative energy technologies.</li>\r\n<li>The auto industry, oil companies and commercial real estate developers would be hurt.</li>\r\n<li>The electronics industry, computer companies and the communications industries would flourish.</li>\r\n<li>Increasingly, workers would own the means of production.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p><em>Observation from today:</em> We are seeing these shifts already. As I sit typing this from home, a 3D printer next to me is producing something (incredibly slowly) that my wife designed. Increasingly, we can create and customize products from home or locally within the community.</p>\r\n<ol start=\"5\">\r\n<li><strong> Radically changed corporations:</strong>The big corporation was the characteristic business organization of the industrial era. Just like families, the mass media and schools, corporations are facing drastic changes:</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>An accelerated economy:</strong> There is a drastic speed-up in the pace of business. An accelerating wave of change, pushed by the coming third wave, is causing disorientation, frustration and increased mistakes on the part of managers.</li>\r\n<li><strong>The de-massified society:</strong> Today, as the third wave strikes, the corporate manager finds all their old assumptions challenged... the marketplace and the labor market are beginning to break into smaller, more varied pieces. Second wave corporations are uncertain how to cope with this rising tide of diversity among their employees and customers.</li>\r\n<li><strong>Public anger at corporations:</strong> People are demanding a new definition of what corporations are and what they do. They want to see more responsibility and more accountability—not merely for its economic performance, but for its side effects on everything from air pollution to executive stress. The result will be corporations who attend to multiple bottom lines. Some examples are already happening as organizations are focusing attention on social impacts as well as economic results.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p><em>Observation from today:</em> Incredibly, those words were written 40 years ago—they read like a modern description of emerging organizations. Just recently, PMI started talking about the triple bottom line (people, profit, planet). Organizations now revolve around the customer, and customer experience analysis is driving more diversification of products along with accelerating rates of change.</p>\r\n<p><strong>What this Means for Project Managers</strong><br />The way we engage with teams is likely to be different in the future. For projects in the knowledge worker space (legal, marketing, sales, education, IT, research and development), having whole teams onsite will likely become a rarity. These roles can be done from the electronic cottage, whether that is someone’s home, a café or a community co-working center.</p>\r\n<p>Work times for project work will likely relax, and family activities take a more influential focus. Industrial factories needed everyone in one location—at the same time—to function. Knowledge work does not; as project managers, we need to get used to that and accommodate for it. Maybe we need everyone to be available for core meetings, but outside of that, we let people work when they want to. As long as they meet their commitments, why does it matter when the work gets done?</p>\r\n<p><strong>The Future</strong><br />Not all of Toffler’s predictions became true. He also suggested we would be growing a significant proportion of our food needs in the oceans. That might be possible, but we have not seen it happening yet.</p>\r\n<p>The second wave of industrialization brought tremendous economic growth and technological development. Yet those brief 300 years were non-sustainable to the planet—and also ripped people from homes and family structures that had existed for 10,000 years previously. Nobody is suggesting we return to being farmers. Instead, do more creative work without the time and space constraints industrialized work demanded.</p>\r\n<p>It seems the world of work is changing to meet Toffler’s predictions. Perhaps the social forecasts about a revival of putting down roots, staying in one place and returning to live with extended families will happen also. Recent events seem to be accelerating these trends, and I am optimistic about our future.</p>\r\n<p><strong><em>References</em></strong></p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Third-Wave-Alvin-Toffler/dp/0553246984/ref=sr_1_2?dchild=1&amp;keywords=the+third+wave&amp;qid=1589990196&amp;sr=8-2\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">The Third Wave</a></em> Book</li>\r\n<li><a href=\"http://www.criticalthink.info/Phil1301/Wave3lec.htm\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"><em>The Third Wave</em> Book Summary</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alvin_Toffler\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Alvin Toffler Wikipedia Bio</a></li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">[Note: For more articles from Mike Griffiths, visit his blog at <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">www.LeadingAnswers.com</a>. Mike first wrote this article for ProjectManagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/634144/Returning-to-the--Electronic--Cottage\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>]</span></p>\r\n<p> </p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Thu, 02 Jul 2020 15:43:23 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9542d5e200b-300wi","linkMd5":"fdd60313d9ab3ee589b10d15dee02420","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn31@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-57-094_56570cf3a9e225aa.webp","destWidth":300,"destHeight":198,"sourceBytes":74822,"destBytes":15302,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9542d5e200b-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn31@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-57-094_56570cf3a9e225aa.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995272},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Agile 2018 Conference – Unraveling Team Dependencies ","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/06/agile-2018-conference-unraveling-team-dependencies-.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39cecca200b-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Agile_SD2018_600x100_Speaking_FM\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39cecca200b image-full img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39cecca200b-800wi\" title=\"Agile_SD2018_600x100_Speaking_FM\" /></a><br />I am excited to be presenting on the Enterprise Agile track at the <a href=\"https://www.agilealliance.org/agile2018/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Agile 2018</a> conference in San Diego, August 7. I have worked with several organizations this year that had issues with work dependencies between teams. My session called “<strong>Two-Pizza Team Heartburn Relief: Solutions to Team Dependencies</strong>” highlights the shift to global rather than local optimization.</p>\r\n<p>We will investigate dependency problems through animations and simulations and then explore some candidate solutions. Each brings their own issues – if these problems were solvable they would have been already, but the suggestions do help considerably. Here is the description from the conference program:</p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #00007f;\">Small teams are great - until they cause bigger problems than they solve. Small teams can communicate more effectively than large teams. They can leverage face-to-face communications more readily and share tacit knowledge without the need for <em>so much</em> written communication. However, for large endeavours, using many small teams present their own problems. Work dependencies between teams can cause major delays through costly hand-offs, mismatched priorities, and blocked tasks.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #00007f;\">This workshop introduces strategies for structuring teams to reduce hand-offs and dependencies that create blocked work and delays. By investigating the (lack of) flow through multiple teams we can diagnose the cost of hand-offs and culprits of delays. We examine tools for making hand-offs and dependencies visible to highlight and bring collective attention to the problems. We then explore resolution patterns and work structures that maximize small team communications but limit negative aspects of managing multiple, inter-dependent project teams.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #00007f;\"><strong>Learning Objectives </strong></span></p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><span style=\"color: #00007f;\">Understand the time and cost penalties of team dependencies and hand-offs</span></li>\r\n<li><span style=\"color: #00007f;\">Gain tools for making dependencies, queues, and blocked work visible</span></li>\r\n<li><span style=\"color: #00007f;\">Learn how and when to balance small team benefits with more dependency issues</span></li>\r\n<li><span style=\"color: #00007f;\">Share implementation patterns and strategies to maximize team throughput</span></li>\r\n<li><span style=\"color: #00007f;\">Examine the pros and cons of larger teams, feature teams, and product <em>vs</em>. project development.</span></li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>That probably sounds more technical than it really is. It is a workshop to show people how teams often get stuck with work items when they rely on work from other groups. It combines anecdotes and experiences from 20+ years of agile consulting along with some insights from Troy Magennis on dependency delays, and Dominica DeGrandis, author of <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Making-Work-Visible-Exposing-Optimize-ebook/dp/B076BYZ6VN\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Making Work Visible</em></a>.</p>\r\n<p>Through case studies and exercises, we explore the hidden impacts of well-intentioned small teams. First, we’ll explore the “mostly harmless” two and three team dependencies, and then see the impacts when five or six dependant teams try to get work done. Please come along if you are attending the conference and have issues with dependencies between teams.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Fri, 29 Jun 2018 15:24:20 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39cecca200b-800wi","linkMd5":"c38573574fb96729c6ce2bad9b09a516","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn88@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-49-56-078_e83fd3f6ffd3781e.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":100,"sourceBytes":56713,"destBytes":12606,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39cecca200b-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn88@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-49-56-078_e83fd3f6ffd3781e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995275},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Creed Over Greed: Motivation and Purpose","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/08/creed-over-greed-motivation-and-purpose.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3636c70200c-pi\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"Sunrise\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3636c70200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3636c70200c-320wi\" style=\"margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"Sunrise\" /></a>There have been a couple of stories in the news recently that reveal some important facts about motivation and purpose.</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Ex-Tesla Workers Are Still Believers</strong></li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>Some people’s reactions over being fired from Tesla surprised many analysts. Rather than the normal angry barbs (and I am sure there were plenty of those) what made the news were the messages of thanks from, now ex-employees, explaining how they enjoyed their time there and were glad to be a part of it. Some of the Tweets included:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><em>“Thanks for the opportunity, Elon! Eye on the mission. Will always be proud to say I worked for Tesla”</em></li>\r\n<li><em>“I just wanted to let you know that I really enjoyed working for Tesla”</em></li>\r\n<li><em>“I was laid off from Tesla yesterday and although it hurts (a lot!), it is the right thing for the company. I don’t regret giving all I had and in a way bidding adieu is my last contribution. I’ll be cheering Tesla on knowing I did my part. Thanks for the years of memories!”</em></li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>A Bloomberg article <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2018-06-13/thanks-for-firing-me-ex-tesla-workers-praise-musk-on-the-way-out\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Fired Tesla Workers Still Love Elon Musk</a> recaps some of the comments.</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Enlightened Pessimism</strong></li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>From a reverse perspective, a recent <a href=\"https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S074959781630646X\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Science Direct</a> article found that <strong>Employees who practice mindfulness meditation are less motivated, having realized the futility of their jobs.</strong> It seems that when people learn how to detach from sources of stress they are less likely to want to work towards goals they are not aligned with.</p>\r\n<p>So, beware those corporate mindfulness workshops unless your organization has a compelling purpose!</p>\r\n<p><strong>The Importance of a Compelling Purpose</strong></p>\r\n<p>First, people want jobs that satisfy their physical and safety needs of having enough money to provide the necessary food and shelter. These are levels one and two in Maslow’s hierarchy of needs. None of the later stages of motivation ever come into play until these most critical needs are met. However, once they are met, people want to work towards something worthwhile and motivating.</p>\r\n<p>Tesla has never been about making fancy electric cars, that’s just a side effect of their real purpose. The Tesla vision and mission statement used to be: “<strong>To accelerate the world's transition to sustainable transport.</strong>” However, in mid-2016, the company changed it to “<strong>To accelerate the world's transition to sustainable energy.</strong>” So, they are not building cars, they are helping save the planet for us and future generations.</p>\r\n<p>That is a worthy goal. It is a purpose people can get behind, and a reason people were glad to work at Tesla, even if their role has now ended. They were never just car makers, they were game changers and that’s what people are grateful for.</p>\r\n<p>Contrast it to the mission of BMW: <strong>“Strategy Number ONE aligns the BMW Group with two targets: to be profitable and to enhance long-term value – from a technological, structural and cultural perspective. The mission statement up to the year 2020 is to become the world's leading provider of premium products and premium services for individual mobility.”</strong></p>\r\n<p>While it mentions culture, there is a focus on profit, value and being the world’s leading… In other words, it is based on money and dominance, rather than a compelling purpose.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Creed Over Greed</strong></p>\r\n<p>Most organizations share mission and vision statements like BMW’s. They talk about generating shareholder value and becoming the biggest this or the leading that. This is understandable in a purely economic model, but as we saw earlier, once people have enough money they want something more - something compelling, something worthwhile.</p>\r\n<p>When we can appeal to people’s desire for meaning, and when we can support them to make valuable contributions to a worthwhile purpose, they will experience motivation beyond the economics that dwindles over time. “Creed” means a belief system, it is more powerful than greed. Growing larger for the sake of profit and market share is unfulfilling, like a cancerous growth.</p>\r\n<p>Having a worthwhile purpose people can unite behind is tremendously powerful. Organizations like <a href=\"https://www.toms.ca/improving-lives\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">TOMS Shoes</a> and <a href=\"https://ca.warbyparker.com/history\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Warby Parker</a> attract top talent not only because they are rewarding places to work, but also because they share a larger goal of helping others less fortunate. Studies show <a href=\"https://www.huffingtonpost.com/brady-josephson/want-to-be-happier-give-m_b_6175358.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">that contributing to good causes makes us happy</a> so it should be no surprise that working for an organization that helps others should be the most rewarding and motivating.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Talent is More Mobile Than Ever</strong></p>\r\n<p>The internet has lowered the cost of communication. It is easier than ever to advertise jobs and share the corporate purpose. People tend to switch jobs more frequently now and the same tools that make advertising jobs easier, also make relocation easier. Smart, talented people are more mobile than ever. They want to apply their skills in worthwhile, interesting settings.</p>\r\n<p>Given the choice of making more money for executives and shareholders or saving the planet, most people (thankfully) would choose to try and help save the planet. When Tesla was hiring last year, they received nearly 500,000 applicants for about 2,500 job openings. So, people only had about a <a href=\"https://electrek.co/2018/03/21/tesla-applicants-employee-growth/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">1 in 200</a> chance of being accepted – a  testimony to how much people want to work there.</p>\r\n<p>With these odds only the very best people get accepted. This has a two-fold effect, 1) the top talent moves to the better companies, 2) lacklustre organizations get a higher concentration of sub-par people as the best move on.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Find the Purpose/Make a Purpose</strong></p>\r\n<p>If you are a CEO, aligning your organization with a higher propose will help attract top talent. If you are a leader in a traditional organization, then creating opportunities for employees to contribute to society is a powerful motivator.</p>\r\n<p>We cannot all work for Tesla or Patagonia, but we can try to inject some worthwhile components into people’s work lives.  Hackathons for a good cause, Habitat for Humanity volunteering, they all help create more satisfied and motivated team members.</p>\r\n<p>At various stages of people’s careers, they care about different things. Many people starting out just want the highest paying job they can obtain to get established in their adult lives. This is understandable and natural. Then, later they want to be part of something bigger, something more useful. Understanding and recognizing this desire allows organizations offering a motivating purpose the capability to appeal to the top tier talent.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[Note: I wrote this article for ProjectManagement.com first and it was published <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/476871/Creed-Over-Greed\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>]</span></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Thu, 16 Aug 2018 13:28:18 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3636c70200c-320wi","linkMd5":"098cb0df087ef91007d1dc2b37a7c47e","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn31@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-953_fcf0369871aa0a2e.webp","destWidth":320,"destHeight":160,"sourceBytes":39331,"destBytes":3710,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3636c70200c-320wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn31@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-953_fcf0369871aa0a2e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"New PM - The What?, Why?, and How? of Project Charters","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/03/new-pm-the-what-why-and-how-of-project-charters.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4460aa2200c-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"Project Charter\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4460aa2200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4460aa2200c-250wi\" style=\"width: 250px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"Project Charter\" /></a>Creating a great project charter is an art and a science. Anyone new to the profession of project management needs to learn how to create a project charter. It is not only an important early project deliverable, but it also sets the tone and lays out the foundation for the rest of the project.</p>\r\n<p>While we can spend our careers improving our ability to craft effective project charters, we can get to a 70% good-enough state by addressing some basic topics. This article explains those basics.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Context is Crucial</strong><br />First, it is critical to understand that context matters. The definition of what makes an acceptable—or great—project charter will vary from organization to organization. It will also be driven by factors such as project size, criticality, type, approach, etc.</p>\r\n<p>The project charter for kicking off a safety-critical public works project will be very different than a charter for a small internal project to, say, build a tool to recover disk space used by duplicate files.</p>\r\n<p>Large, critical projects will require large comprehensive charters. These can take teams of experts weeks or months to create. Small projects will likely have their three- to eight-page charter written in a day or two by the project manager. When creating (or reviewing) project charters, we need to understand this context. Ask, what is appropriate? What level of rigor and detail does this effort require and deserve?</p>\r\n<p>To start the chartering process, we first need to understand a few things about the project goals and our internal processes.</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li>For the project, we need to understand the business case and an outline of the desired scope.</li>\r\n<li>For our organization, we must understand any strategic plans we need to align with, our standards and processes, contracts to use, and any relevant external factors like market conditions and industry standards.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>Once we know these things, we can start writing our charter.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Make it Clear</strong><br />I am a simple person and like simple ideas and definitions. I probably miss subtle nuances but have learned that most people appreciate simple, clear documents. The style points we lose for a lack of sophistication are made up for by improved comprehension and clarity. So, my definition of a project charter is a document that authorizes the project and explains the <em>what</em>,<em> why</em>,<em> where</em>,<em> when</em>,<em> who </em>and<em> how</em> aspects of the project.</p>\r\n<p>We can call the <em>what</em>, <em>why</em>, <em>where</em>, <em>when</em>, <em>who</em> questions W5 and add a “+” for the final <em>how</em> question. Provided the project charter answers the W5+ questions and provides approval to start the project—all at the appropriate level of rigor—it’s a winner. So, let’s get started by reviewing each question…</p>\r\n<p><strong>What?</strong><br />In your project charter, you will not call the section “What?” It will likely be called “Scope” or something similar. The “W5+” idea is just a tool to make sure we address the important sections. So, in the scope section, we would describe or list the major deliverables or high-level functionality the project should deliver. People need to understand what we are talking about before they can appreciate further details such as schedule and approach.</p>\r\n<p>When defining what the project will deliver, it is also useful to state what it will not deliver. So, a list of “out of scope” items is also valuable. It is better to have sponsors or user representatives complain now rather than halfway through the project (or worse, at completion when there is nothing we can do about it) that their anticipated element will not be delivered.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Why?</strong><br />The <em>why</em> of a project is described in a “Problem Statement” or “Business Need” section. Some people put this section before the <em>what</em>. That is fine; follow your organization’s standard, or the preferred sequence of who approves project charters (or failing those, your own preference). Just make sure the <em>what</em> and the <em>why</em> are addressed early on.</p>\r\n<p>People need to understand why this project is important. What new revenue will it bring? What problem or legal requirement does it serve? What new opportunities, new products or new customers do we hope it will attract? Projects are expensive and risky endeavors, so we better have a good reason to undertake one. The business case or problem statement is where we describe it.</p>\r\n<p>It needs to be clear and compelling. It may reference a separate business case or return on investment analysis. If these components are necessary and not in separate documents, include a summary in the charter body and put the details in an appendix. We don’t want people to stop reading our project charter because they came across pages of calculations.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Where?</strong><br />“Where?” can seem a strange question, maybe inserted just to get the “W” count up to five. However, think broader. Which markets, products, and departments are we impacting? Where is the project going to touch our organization, customer base, and market segment?</p>\r\n<p>Remember: The project charter normally provides approval for a project to start. We need to provide the relevant context so people are thinking appropriately before they approve, reject or request changes be made.</p>\r\n<p>This information may be contained in a section called “Organizational Impact” or form a sub-section of the scope section. It also explains how the project is aligned with the organization’s strategic plan.</p>\r\n<p><strong>When?</strong><br />This is where we describe the “Project schedule”—not only when we plan to start and (hopefully) complete the project, but also major steps along the way.</p>\r\n<p>Being a project manager writing a charter, it’s easy to get caught up in the apparent importance of your project. You might assume that once approved, the organization will rush full-steam ahead into kickoff and execution. We need to inject some caution and realism. What seems important and obvious to us is often low priority for the sponsors or those already over capacity in executing existing projects and operations. Things might take a while to get going.</p>\r\n<p>So, do not build a schedule based on starting work immediately after approval. That just sets everyone up for failure. The most common source of late project completions is not poor estimating or a lack of risk management, it is late starts. Every late project I have reviewed had a late start. They may also have been terrible at estimating and blind to common threats, but late starts are very common. We need to explain that real end dates are driven by real start dates.</p>\r\n<p>Projects get delayed for a host of common reasons. We were delayed in getting our team, we were delayed in finding a space, we were delayed in accessing funds. So, do yourself (and everybody else) a favor and explain that the project will likely take three weeks, months or years from having the requisite start conditions.</p>\r\n<p>We also need to be realistic about uncertainty. The uncertainty associated with our estimates needs to be reflected (to some degree) in our schedule. It is probably not acceptable to say, “We have no clue when we will be done.” But do not commit to completing within a certain timeframe unless you have a realistic and robust plan for achieving that.</p>\r\n<p>Robust means including contingency to address uncertainty. Be open about it, such as, “We included a 15% buffer for unanticipated work.” You might get asked to remove it and “work smarter” or “find a way, damn it!” and that is fine. You reflected the uncertainty inherent with the work. Depending how supportive the sponsors are, we could consider explaining that removing contingency is accepting the risk of an overrun due to learning in the future things that we do not know today.</p>\r\n<p>Plans and estimates created at the beginning of the project are, by definition, the least reliable because that is when we know the least about the project. It is only when we begin to execute that we learn about its true complexity and the actual abilities of the team, vendors and supporting groups. Sponsors usually don’t want to hear this kind of smartass insolence from the project managers. PMs are hired to deliver projects, not tell them how to set stretch goals or run a business.</p>\r\n<p>There are other ways to shorten a schedule. We can cut the scope of what is delivered. This could allow us to hit a deadline and maybe have a follow-on release for lower-priority work. It is not ideal and is really wriggling out of the defined scope. However, for software products, where must-have and nice-to-have features are more fluid, it could be a viable option.</p>\r\n<p>We can also add more people to the project. This works great if you are undertaking simple work like digging ditches or building pyramids. For anything more complex that involves problem-solving, idea sharing and collaboration, books like <em>The Mythical Man-Month</em> explain that adding people to a project that is late will make it later (while spending more, too).</p>\r\n<p>For these reasons (and others learned the hard way), make sure schedules clearly contain contingency to reflect uncertainties. Also, ensure that schedules work from a project start date that is contingent upon having prerequisite project conditions in place. Yes, they might both get ignored, but it is the responsible thing to do—and you can bring them up at steering committee meetings when asked why the project is behind.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Who?</strong><br />The who question represents the “Team” and “Stakeholder” sections of a project charter. It is normal to show org charts of core project roles and list known team members and open positions. RACI charts can be used to list who is <strong>r</strong>esponsible, <strong>a</strong>ccountable, <strong>c</strong>onsulted and <strong>i</strong>nformed (RACI) for work and deliverables.</p>\r\n<p>We should understand that the term <em>stakeholder</em> encompasses not only the people working on the project and sponsoring it, but also everyone it will impact. This extended family of project influencers include suppliers, customers, and even the general public if the project is likely to draw public opinion. Obviously, we do not list these broader communities by name, but we should identify them and assign a contact within the project for managing that group.</p>\r\n<p>The PMI definition of a stakeholder includes not only those impacted by the project, but even those who perceive they may be impacted by the project. This is important—the scope of people who may influence development is wide. It is better to have a plan for engaging or at least monitoring these groups (be them environmental, minority or special interest) before they can blindside the project. Inventing a fire-response plan is always easier to do before you also have a fire on your hands.</p>\r\n<p><strong>How?</strong><br />The <em>how</em> question reminds us to explain the “Project Approach.” We should describe how the project will be executed. Will we be following the standard corporate project lifecycle? Are we trying a new approach? Are we outsourcing portions?</p>\r\n<p>People need to understand how the project will be executed before agreeing to fund it or participate in it. If they think the project has merit but we are suggesting to go about it all wrong, they will want the ability to influence the approach used.</p>\r\n<p>When we are following a standard approach, it is sufficient to just name it. When we are proposing something different, we need to describe it in more detail. This could be a reference to another document or pointer to an appendix in our project charter.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Approval</strong><br />The charter describes the project from a holistic perspective by addressing the W5+ questions; it also provides the approval/authorization to start work. In most organizations, approval of the charter triggers a request for funds or authorization for expenditure (AFE). For this reason, we need some formality around the approval and sign-off of the charter. It is normal to have a signature section for sponsor, division leads and other steering committee members.</p>\r\n<p>The approval circumstances are rarely as simple as either approved or not approved. It is usual to have definitions of the various options that the steering committee may use. Common status options include:</p>\r\n<p><em><strong>Approve</strong></em> – Looks good, let’s get started<br /><em><strong>Approve with modifications</strong></em> – Will be okay if you make these changes (provide some space in the signature area to hand-write requested changes and then get signatures)<br /><em><strong>Request changes</strong></em> – Major changes are required and a new charter will need to be submitted<br /><em><strong>Defer</strong></em> – Not at this time, keep on hold<br /><em><strong>Reject</strong></em> – No, do not proceed</p>\r\n<p><strong>What about agile projects that do not have charters?</strong><br />Today’s agile projects produce fewer documents. However, since charters often authorize work to start and trigger the release of funds, we still sometimes see them used in hybrid traditional/agile environments. If project charters are not used in name, typically a lighter-weight deliverable with a different name is used.</p>\r\n<p>We might see an <a href=\"https://projectresources.cdt.ca.gov/agile/the-agile-project-charter/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Agile Charter</a>, <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/228664/Visionate--The-What---Part-1-----Capturing-the-Sponsor-s-Vision\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Project Skinny</a> or <a href=\"https://medium.com/qdivision/the-product-canvas-edf8df531\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Product Canvas</a>. The purpose is similar—describe the endeavor and get agreement to start. When working with agile approaches, we can still use the W5+ idea to make sure we address the common viewpoints. The coverage in each section will likely be brief, but is still helpful.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Summary</strong><br />Consider the context you are working in. Organizational standards and project characteristics such as size, cost, and impact of failure will dictate the level of detail and rigor that will be necessary. Then keep the sections simple and clear. Use appendices or references to external documents if sections become too long. We want people to be able to read the core body of our charter through in one sitting and then make a decision to approve it.</p>\r\n<p>Make sure the sections address the W5+ views of the project one topic at a time. For instance, do not mix schedule with business justification; keep them separate. Do not paint yourself into a corner by committing to unrealistic delivery dates or optimistic costs. Present what you believe is realistic and let the steering committee assume the risks of reduction (if possible).</p>\r\n<p>Recommending a template is problematic since organizational needs vary, but common sections for a small- to medium-sized project might contain:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Introduction</strong> – Explain the purpose of the charter</li>\r\n<li><strong>Problem Statement</strong> – Outline the <em>what</em></li>\r\n<li><strong>Scope Outline</strong> – More detail on <em>what</em> would be delivered</li>\r\n<li><strong>Definition of Success</strong> – Define what “done” would look like</li>\r\n<li><strong>Risk Summary</strong> – Review the high-level threats and opportunities identified</li>\r\n<li><strong>Constraints and Assumptions</strong> – Outline the accepted operating parameters used</li>\r\n<li><strong>Business Case</strong> – Explain <em>why</em> the organization should do this</li>\r\n<li><strong>Schedule</strong> – Explain <em>when</em> the project will be completed</li>\r\n<li><strong>Deliverables Schedule</strong> – Outline <em>when</em> the key deliverables will be completed</li>\r\n<li><strong>Budget</strong> – Explain <em>how</em> much this is likely to cost</li>\r\n<li><strong>Team Structure</strong> – Outline <em>who</em> will be working on the project     </li>\r\n<li><strong>Organizational Structure</strong> – Explain <em>who</em> will be responsible for oversight and direction</li>\r\n<li><strong>Project Approach</strong> – Explain <em>how</em> the project will be run</li>\r\n<li><strong>Steering Committee Decision</strong> – Record the approval (or otherwise) of the charter</li>\r\n<li><strong>Appendices</strong></li>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><em>Project Background</em> – Supporting material about <em>why </em>this is a good thing to do</li>\r\n<li><em>Deliverables List</em> – A list of <em>what</em> should be delivered and what Done looks like for each</li>\r\n<li><em>Deliverables Schedule</em> – A schedule for the deliverables’ leave some wiggle room</li>\r\n<li><em>Risk Assessment Detail</em> – Details of the threats and opportunities analyzed to date</li>\r\n<li><em>Communication Plan</em> – Details of <em>how</em> people will be kept informed about progress and issues</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[Note: I first wrote this article for ProjectManagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/521846/The-What--Why-and-How-of-Project-Charters\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a> ]</span></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Thu, 14 Mar 2019 23:39:28 My email is Mike &lt;at&gt; LeadingAnswers.com</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Mon, 04 May 2020 16:01:48 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9452edb200b-250wi","linkMd5":"564f5df6a08fc5d56f626ba824d06caa","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn64@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-682_18e468b23d5a0df8.webp","destWidth":250,"destHeight":245,"sourceBytes":15636,"destBytes":6150,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9452edb200b-250wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn64@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-682_18e468b23d5a0df8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Agile Animations","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/09/agile-animations.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad369343a200c-pi\"><img alt=\"Animation Film\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad369343a200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad369343a200c-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Animation Film\" /></a><br />Following my Agile 2018 conference workshop, I had a couple of people ask how I created the smooth PowerPoint animations. I have always liked using animations to explain ideas since they help me understand processes.</p>\r\n<p>My logic has been, if they help me understand it, then they should help others understand it too. Visual learning, and especially animations, are valuable on knowledge work projects.</p>\r\n<p>Animations help us overcome the three challenges of knowledge work:</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><strong>Invisible</strong> – designs and ideas are often abstract and hard to visualize.</li>\r\n<li><strong>Intangible</strong> – bits not atoms. Since we cannot see or feel ideas there is a real danger other people might interpret them differently, leading to difficulties with collaboration and problem-solving.</li>\r\n<li><strong>Transient</strong> – Our work is often novel and unique, the challenges teams face are often unique too. The solution to our last problem is unlikely to help us today.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>Tom Wujec, the author of <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Future-Making-Tom-Wujec/dp/1595910948/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1536274375&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=The+Future+of+Making\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">The Future of Making</a>, has an interesting <a href=\"https://youtu.be/wPFA8n7goio?t=51\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">short Ted talk</a> on how animation helps create meaning. He explains seeing an image triggers 30 portions of the brain to start working together to process information, solve problems and make decisions.</p>\r\n<p>Visualizations address the knowledge worker challenges:</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><strong>Clarity through visualization</strong> – engage all those brain circuits, helping us comprehend faster and clarify ideas.</li>\r\n<li><strong>Making concepts interactive</strong> – when we all see the same interaction of components, we build a common understanding as a group.</li>\r\n<li><strong>Make permanent</strong> – Animations can be stored, shared, and replayed - capturing mental models of A-ha moments.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>So, if a picture is worth a thousand words, is an animation worth a thousand pictures (a million words)? – No, but it is hard to beat visual storytelling.</p>\r\n<p>Here are some of the animations I used. I was explaining that dependencies and handoffs between teams are costly. We first looked at the simple agile environment everyone learns about where teams pull work from their own backlog with no cross-team dependencies.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3693290200c-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"Single Team\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3693290200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3693290200c-800wi\" style=\"width: 800px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Single Team\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>We then looked at 3 teams with dependencies. If some work must be done by other teams, e.g. waiting for an environment team or microservices team, then handoffs occur. Tasks are passed to those teams for completion. However, those teams are busy doing their own work, so these requests usually get placed low in their backlog. Then the original team explains the urgency and negotiates reprioritization.</p>\r\n<p>This all takes time and effort. While Team 1 is waiting for Team 2 to do their work they don’t want to forget about it, so they usually clone the user story and keep it visible until completed. It creates the familiar dance of follow-up and horse-trading we often see on projects.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdc0200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"Multiple Teams\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdc0200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdc0200b-800wi\" style=\"width: 800px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Multiple Teams\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>Teams with a couple of dependencies like this are usually OK. My workshop was about how to cope when we have many more dependencies. Organizations that outsource portions of work, have specialized service groups, or handoffs to support teams create nightmarish tangles of inefficiencies. They are so entangled in handoffs and dependencies that tracking throughput or value-delivery metrics are pointless - other than to show how poor they are.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdcc200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"Blocked 1\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdcc200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdcc200b-800wi\" style=\"width: 800px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Blocked 1\" /></a></p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad38f576e200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"Blocked 2\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad38f576e200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad38f576e200d-800wi\" style=\"width: 800px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Blocked 2\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>The workshop examined how to bring visibility to the problem and introduce solutions. We simulated Handoff-Hell with a table exercise that required inputs from other participants. We then explored resolution patterns that kept small-team benefits but limited the problems of multiple, inter-dependent project teams. We wrapped up explaining how to transition to a Product vs Project structure when you have many in-flight teams.</p>\r\n<p>However, this post is about creating Agile Animations. If you are stuck in Handoff-Hell contact me about how to fix it. If you want to learn about creating animations to illustrate agile principles read-on, or rather, watch-on. I realized it would be contradictory to write about visual learning and how to create animations. If we truly learn best visually, and movement shows action better than words, I really need a video.</p>\r\n<p>So, after 12 years of just writing on this site, here’s my first video. <strong>How to Create Agile Animations in PowerPoint</strong>.</p>\r\n<p>(This was not easy for me to make. I am an introvert who does not like to hear myself recorded, so it is outside my comfort zone. However, I believe video is the future for education and I had to start somewhere.)</p>\r\n<p>Hopefully, after explaining simple topics I can go onto tackle more advanced ones.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p /></p>\r\n<p> </p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Fri, 07 Sep 2018 03:02:07 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad369343a200c-600wi","linkMd5":"102955c29f79f18721340c5b98af4338","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn80@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-49-55-787_91c52c0bd0462af1.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":267,"sourceBytes":157095,"destBytes":18036,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad369343a200c-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn80@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-49-55-787_91c52c0bd0462af1.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3693290200c-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn92@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-50-15-596_cbf6ff9eff4d65f2.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdc0200b-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn59@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-50-18-596_d817276373112813.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3aefdcc200b-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn98@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-484_48c90fd113ae4cea.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad38f576e200d-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn3@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-50-07-115_b71d6dfe5fb51254.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"WBS and Product Backlog: Siblings or Distant Cousins?","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2020/03/wbs-and-product-backlog-siblings-or-distant-cousins.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a98200c-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"WBSandPB\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a98200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a98200c-300wi\" style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"WBSandPB\" /></a>It’s easy to believe that work breakdown structures (WBS) have been around since the pyramids were built in Egypt, and that product backlogs are new inventions by youngsters in too much of a hurry to plan correctly. However, like most things, the truth is more complicated.</p>\r\n<p>In 1957, the Program Evaluation and Review Technique (PERT) approach was created by the United States Department of Defense (DoD) and described organizing tasks into product-oriented categories. However, they did not use the term “work breakdown structure” or WBS until 1962 when DoD, NASA and the aerospace industry published a document about PERT that described the WBS approach.</p>\r\n<p>Meanwhile, in 1960, Tom Gilb described his Evolutionary Value Delivery approach (or Evo for short) that is widely accepted to be a forerunner of agile approaches. Evo contains principles such as:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>E1: Decompose by performance results and stakeholders</strong> –  Break down the work into small (weekly) value delivery steps</li>\r\n<li><strong>E2: Do high-risk steps early</strong> – Prioritize the work based on risk</li>\r\n<li><strong>E3: Focus on improving your most valuable objectives firs</strong>t – Also prioritize the work based on business value</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>These ideas became the concepts embodied in backlogs by today’s agile approaches and frameworks.</p>\r\n<p>So, we can trace each approach back to around the same time and also be confident these ideas were firmly established long before that. Building the pyramids and Roman cities required multiple levels of planning, work decomposition and task coordination. There is little point in arguing whether WBSs or backlogs came first since it was clearly lots of other things.</p>\r\n<p>These days, the PMI <a href=\"https://www.pmi.org/pmbok-guide-standards/framework/practice-standard-work-breakdown-structures-3rd-edition\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Practice Standard for Work Breakdown Structures</a> defines a WBS as <em>“…a hierarchical decomposition of the total scope of work to be carried out by the project team to accomplish the project objectives and create the desired deliverables.</em>” The Scrum Guide defines a product backlog as “<em>an ordered list of everything that is known to be needed in the product.</em>”</p>\r\n<p>Are they really so different? They both help with forming an agreement on scope. Yet, due to how they are often used, they are viewed as quite different by many people…a viewpoint I would like to change.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a51298ee200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"WBS and Backlog\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a51298ee200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a51298ee200b-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"WBS and Backlog\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>WBS and Backlog Similarities and Differences</strong><br />Work breakdown structures are often defined upfront and supported with a WBS dictionary. They can be used to form statements of work and contracts. If these deliverables are useful for your projects, then great, create them. However, understand we could create the same deliverables from a product backlog also.</p>\r\n<p>Yet, on agile projects, we typically do not because these environments tend to be more dynamic so these deliverables would soon be out of date. Instead, we create iteration plans, release roadmaps and work from the top of the backlog while the product owner manages the backlog with evolving priorities and change requests. These deliverables are easier to update as changes occur. The differences we see in action stem more from the characteristics of the work environment than the WBS or backlog tool in use. Both help us define and discuss scope.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Visual Benefits</strong><br />Both are visual and allow us to point at items as we talk about them. This is critically important. Visual depictions of work allow us to collaborate more effectively. When two people face a task board or WBS diagram, they can collaborate with less contention. Visualizations help us build shared understandings and avoid confusions such as having two similar items being considered the same thing or assuming one solution fits two scenarios when it does not.</p>\r\n<p>Depicting scope visually also allows us to shade and color items to indicate type, ownership, risk and completion status.  Visualizing work is a major component of lean thinking. When we visualize something, we also process information using more of our brain—and much more quickly compared to reading and interpreting written information. This is one reason we have road signs with images and not descriptions to read. It is the difference between decoding and understanding meaning in 150 microseconds (roads signs) versus 6,000 microseconds for reading:</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a01200c-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Signpost\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a01200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a01200c-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"Signpost\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Product Backlogs as a form of WBS</strong><br />The third edition of the Practice Standard for Work Breakdown Structures talks about backlogs as a form of WBS. Many people think only tree structures of boxes and lines are work breakdown structures, but they can actually take on many forms including tabular backlogs or even mind maps.</p>\r\n<p>I can imagine some purists shouting “No, that’s not a WBS!” as I type this, but go check it out for yourself if you do not believe me. The Practice Standard for Work Breakdown Structures WBS is a free download for PMI members. </p>\r\n<p>I participated as a reviewer for the standard and was pleased to see its coverage of agile scope decomposition using epics, features, user stories and tasks as candidate WBS elements. One thing that puzzled me that I am hoping a reader of this article can help me with is the inclusion of sprints or iterations as potential WBS elements.</p>\r\n<p>My confusion stems from the logical definition of work in section 2.1 that says <em>“… work refers to outputs, work products, or deliverables that are the results of effort, not the effort itself</em>.” – that makes sense. Then in section 2.3.1.1 on WBS rules it says “<em>WBS elements do not account for time or sequence.</em>” Again, that seems reasonable.</p>\r\n<p>However, the examples for agile projects include a WBS with level 2 items showing “Iteration 1,” “Iteration 2,” etc. containing work. This seems to violate the deliverables versus effort definition of “work and no-time” rule. We would not have a WBS element called “September,” so why call out some arbitrary time box? Iterations are just time constructs, and you might choose to use them or work without them as a continuous pull of features from the backlog.</p>\r\n<p>Likely I am interpreting the work, deliverables and no-time rules too literally and, like debating which approach came first,  it probably does not matter. If having iterations shown helps you share your plans and have meaningful conversations about scope, then go for it. I would imagine it would result in having to refactor the WBS frequently as stories get shifted between iterations as priorities change and throughput varies, but maybe not.</p>\r\n<p>The more important idea is that we visualize and discuss project scope with a wide variety of stakeholders to surface and correct misunderstandings. The good news is that product backlogs are a legitimate form of WBS and more of a sibling than a distant cousin. A couple of great quotes from the WBS Practice Standard reiterates the value and applies equally well to backlogs and release roadmaps:</p>\r\n<p>“<em>The WBS provides the foundation for a visual representation of the scope of work…Research demonstrates that communication is one of the project management disciplines with the highest impact on project success. The WBS serves as a critical project communication mechanism that helps convey the scope of the project through its graphical depiction.</em>”</p>\r\n<p>So let’s get graphical and keep communicating.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">[Note: This article first appeared on ProjectManagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/606176/WBS-and-Product-Backlog--Siblings-or-Distant-Cousins-\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>. For more articles from Mike Griffiths check his blog <a href=\"http://www.LeadingAnswers.com\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">www.LeadingAnswers.com</a>]</span></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Tue, 03 Mar 2020 17:43:15 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a98200c-300wi","linkMd5":"2188704971c00e8796314a558e6fa2f1","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn22@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-55-731_e16a49b243507c4e.webp","destWidth":300,"destHeight":202,"sourceBytes":53912,"destBytes":9458,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a98200c-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn22@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-55-731_e16a49b243507c4e.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a51298ee200b-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn3@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-50-05-736_80d0fd5fd938e7ae.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2025d9b386a01200c-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn84@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-741_ea8e0567606183be.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"My Work-From-Home Mistakes","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2020/06/my-work-from-home-mistakes.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p style=\"text-align: center; margin-top: 0cm; background: white;\"><strong><em><span style=\"font-size: 10.5pt; font-family: 'Helvetica',sans-serif; color: #575757;\">“It could be that the purpose of your life is only to serve as a warning to others.”</span></em></strong><span style=\"font-size: 10.5pt; font-family: 'Helvetica',sans-serif; color: #575757;\"><br style=\"box-sizing: border-box;\" />– Despair calendar quote</span></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e951c39a200b-pi\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"Mistakes\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20263e951c39a200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e951c39a200b-150wi\" style=\"width: 150px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"Mistakes\" /></a>Today I am going to get some work done. I am because yesterday, to be honest, was not that productive. It started with the best intentions. Without a commute, I was at my computer by 7:30 a.m., earlier than my usual start time at the office. Pleased with my head start, I rewarded myself with a read through my news feeds.</p>\r\n<p>I use a news aggregator to collect all my science, sports, local interest, photography and project management articles and announcements in one place. Most items I just scan in the aggregator platform, only clicking into the full articles for relevant or exciting topics.</p>\r\n<p>Before I knew it, it is 8:40. Crap, there goes my early start…time to buckle down. Reading and replying to emails takes me to my first phone meeting. It is slow and lumbering, but mercifully finishes 10 minutes before the one hour allotted. I go and have a coffee with my wife.</p>\r\n<p>The coffee was good, so was the toast and chocolate cookie. When I return, I have more emails, but I get through them and open up my “real” work. Having three monitors now is fantastic, but my new monitor has a higher resolution than the others so when I drag documents to it the text is too small. Maybe I should just set it to the same resolution as the other two? Thirty minutes later, I have all three monitors running updated video drivers at the same resolution and a fancy new screen saver that flows across all three monitors…sweet. But now my mouse is acting up.</p>\r\n<p>It probably just needs a new battery. There should be one in this drawer. Wow, we need a good clear-out. None of this junk is used anymore; I am going to make a pile of things to keep and a pile of things to throw away. Hey look, an old digital camera…I wonder, what is on the memory card?</p>\r\n<p><strong>Wake up</strong><br />That was an excerpt from my life five years ago when I first started working from home. It was ridiculous; I hardly got any work done. It was actually far worse than that because I had made the switch to work from home in part to live more simply. The idea was to spend more time with my family and enjoy the outdoors in the mountain town we had just moved to. So in addition to endless in-office distractions, I would also take two- to three-hour breaks to go trail running or mountain biking. As a result, I achieved almost nothing.</p>\r\n<p>If you are struggling to get work done from home, do not feel bad. I am confident my procrastination, short attention span, and penchant for anything but work were as bad as it gets. Yet, now I am more productive than ever.</p>\r\n<p>Last year I consulted at several organizations, taught training courses, wrote a book and 40 articles for ProjectManagement.com, developed courseware, presented at numerous conferences and—according to the GPS tracking website Strava—still managed to fit in 460 hours of mountain biking and running.</p>\r\n<p>So, if you are having difficulty focusing while working from home, I think I can help. I know it is difficult with children to help home-school and more significant problems like a global pandemic, but what worked for me will likely work for you too, if you are ready to try it.</p>\r\n<p>I was so frustrated at my lack of focus and inability to get work done that I treated researching the cause and solution as a project. I discovered my work hygiene was to blame. I created new habits, saw the results and now get my work done early. It was not comfortable, but better than the feeling I had of repeatedly achieving practically nothing all day.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Lose the distractions</strong><br />Prolific writer Jon Morrow, tells us: “<em>There is no such thing as focus. There is only an absence of distraction</em><strong><em>.</em></strong>” So, when we remove the distractions, there is nothing else to do but work. The trouble is that distractions come from many sources.</p>\r\n<p><strong>1. Close the door</strong>– I used to have an office off the kitchen. It was great for relaxed conversations, efficient for grabbing a quick coffee—also a nightmare for distractions. I moved my office downstairs and now close the door when I am working. The closed door means I am working. I do not want a coffee; I do not want to see a funny cat video. (I probably do, but I will not; instead, I am working.) Every hour I will come out, be friendly and chat, but when the door is closed, please no interruptions. It sounds harsh, but is nothing compared to what happens behind that closed door; things get much stricter.</p>\r\n<p><strong>2. Personal Pomodoro</strong>– When I do not have meetings, I work on a task for 45 minutes out of an hour. Then spend 15 minutes checking and responding to email, being social with the family, getting snacks and drinks, moving about, etc. For those 45 minutes, I eliminate rather than try to avoid distractions. So for me, this means no cellphone, no email, no internet, no music, no drinks, no snacks, nothing. Only the task and a countdown timer to tell me how much longer to work.</p>\r\n<p>People with more self-control or better multi-tasking skills may not need to be so extreme, but I do. Fidget toys, squeezy balls, paper airplanes—anything that is more fun than a keyboard is banished.</p>\r\n<p>The original Pomodoro technique uses a 25-minute mechanical timer. I tried that followed by a five-minute break two times per hour—but kept overrunning my five-minute breaks. So, I switched to 45 minutes focus, 15 minutes for interruptions and everything else. </p>\r\n<p>This ratio works for me, but something different might work better for you. The timing is less critical than being brutal and obsessive about the elimination of all distractions. After actually working for 45 minutes, I am happy to open my door, chat, mess about with my son, or whatever; now I am no longer anxious about underachieving. <span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">(I like the electronic <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Time-Timer-Charcoal-Operation-Management/dp/B06XKZTCS2/ref=sr_1_5?dchild=1&amp;keywords=Time+Timer+Plus+60+Minute+Visual+Analog+Timer&amp;qid=1592684587&amp;sr=8-5\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Time Timer</a> because it is quiet and the ticking of mechanical timers seemed enough to distract me too.)</span></p>\r\n<p><strong>3. Start Earlier</strong>– By going to bed earlier and getting up earlier, I gain an hour of quiet time in the mornings before everyone else gets up. I no longer waste it reading news feeds, I plan or complete an extra 45-minute work period while there are few distractions.</p>\r\n<p>I check my calendar, plan when I will do my 45-minute chunks based around the meetings I have and readjust to do say a 20-minute session if I have only a 30-minute time slot.</p>\r\n<p><strong>4. Evolve</strong>– My original plan was to be very strict with the no distractions rule until I caught up on my backlog of work. Then I might reintroduce having my phone with me so that I could reply to text messages, or maybe the radio on, or a collaboration website open.</p>\r\n<p>However, my productivity went up so much that I would hate to lose it again. Finally, I get to finish work earlier and get more free time. I just wish I’d discovered 30 years ago how easily I am distracted, how poorly I multi-task and the actual cost of task-switching. For me at least, they are significant.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Different Strokes for Different Folks</strong><br />Your work-from-home situation is different from mine. Your schedule, personal distractions and options for isolation will be unique. However, I expect you can relate to some of the challenges I encountered when I started working from home five years ago. I hope some of the approaches I adopted to solve my productivity problems will help you, too.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">[Note: For more articles from Mike Griffiths, visit his blog at <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/\">www.LeadingAnswers.com</a>.
