conference on November 30<sup>th</sup>. Along with Mary Poppendieck, we will be leading a day dedicated to learning about agile culture and collaboration.</p>\r\n<p>The Gatineau Ottawa Agile Tour is an annual conference in the heart of the nation’s capital, focused on sharing and learning. GOAT has run for 7 years and is part of the Agile Tour that takes place in 90 cities worldwide.</p>\r\n<p>Click to see the <a href=\"https://goagiletour.ca/node/1/keynotes\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Keynotes Overview</a> and the <a href=\"https://goagiletour.ca/node/1/sessions-preview\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Sessions Previews</a>.</p>\r\n<p>I hope to see you there.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Mon, 12 Nov 2018 19:22:16 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39fe7d3200d-250wi","linkMd5":"931deda898ffb450da858a8633ecef66","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn15@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-55-815_42b0674991f0909d.webp","destWidth":250,"destHeight":167,"sourceBytes":63140,"destBytes":10324,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad39fe7d3200d-250wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn15@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-49-55-815_42b0674991f0909d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Review of Product Development Books","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/06/review-of-product-development-books.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19fdc200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"Product Development Cycle\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19fdc200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19fdc200b-350wi\" style=\"width: 350px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"Product Development Cycle\" /></a>Now that a software “Done” Milestone is more like a Tombstone</strong></p>\r\n<p>If you work in an industry that has digital products and services then the Product Development trend will impact you. As software becomes more critical to business operations and product offerings we are seeing that software projects do not end.</p>\r\n<p>Many organizations are transitioning to become software focussed organizations that offer specialized services. Amazon is a software company with retail (and cloud) offerings. Banks are increasingly digital companies with financial services. The same with insurance, travel, music and even commercial goods. The cost of developing the software in new vehicles is now greater than the cost of the engine. It has become the single most expensive component, even in internal combustion engine vehicles with no autonomous driving features.</p>\r\n<p>These websites and software services will only be “done” development when the company stops being competitive, offering new services or keeping up with technology evolution. At one time getting to \"Done\" on your software project was a relief, a goal, a milestone, now it is more of a tombstone. It means the product is no longer competing or actively being maintained as technology continues to evolve.</p>\r\n<p>Switching from projects (that are temporary in nature) to products that are designed to be ongoing sounds easy enough - just keep funding the team, but for many organizations it is not that simple. Also, organizations that embrace the whole digital product view still need help governing the ongoing process.</p>\r\n<p>This is where product development books can help. They describe the factors at play and provide ideas for guidance around planning, funding, staffing and governance. As I was working with clients experiencing the transition from projects to products I was lucky to engage with several authors of <em>product-first, #NoProjects</em> books and chat about the challenge areas and potential solutions.</p>\r\n<p>I have written about <em>#NoProjects</em> a few times before:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2017/01/when-will-this-software-project-ever-be-done-.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">When will this software project ever be done</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/04/where-did-all-the-project-managers-go.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Where did all the project managers go?</a></li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/06/the-truth-about-transformations.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">The truth about transformations</a></li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p>Then when I recently read a third book about product development shift I thought it could be useful to review some of the books in the market. A neat aspect of these books is that they all present different views on the project to product mind-shift and journey.  </p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19f8a200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Continuous Digital\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19f8a200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19f8a200b-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Continuous Digital\" /></a></p>\r\n<p> “<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Continuous-Digital-Allan-Kelly/dp/0993325092/ref=sr_1_1?keywords=allan+kelly+continuous+digital&amp;qid=1560111981&amp;s=gateway&amp;sr=8-1\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>Continuous Digital: An agile alternative to projects for digital business</strong></a>”</p>\r\n<p><strong>Allan Kelly, Software Strategy Ltd.; October 2018</strong></p>\r\n<p>Allan’s book was the first I read about switching to continuous product development. I had been following his blog for a number of years and was familiar with his work. However, it was not until reading the book that I saw his points laid out in order with full explanations.</p>\r\n<p>It is a great read, it makes a compelling argument for why a project view of software projects is a flawed model. I loved the explanation on the diseconomies-of-scale for software and why it is actually cheaper in small batches – unlike physical goods.</p>\r\n<p>It offers the best explanations I have heard into why a continuous delivery of features by a stable team is preferable to using conventional project models. It nicely describes the team aspects of knowledge work and offers some good suggestions around funding and governance models.  </p>\r\n<p>Any change in mindset has to happen internally first before we can help others adopt it. Continuous Digital cemented my own thoughts about why good software projects never end. It explained the “Why?” questions at the heart of any shift in thinking and behavior. In the same way, we have to understand the agile mindset before generating any kind of commitment towards it. This book helps set the mindset and Why of #NoProjects so we can start our journey.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4a8200c-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Noprojects book\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4a8200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4a8200c-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Noprojects book\" /></a>“<a href=\"https://www.infoq.com/minibooks/noprojects-value-culture/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>#noprojects: A Culture of Continuous Value</strong></a>”</p>\r\n<p><strong>Evan Leybourn, Shane Hastie, lulu.com; July 2018</strong></p>\r\n<p>I read an early draft of Evan and Shane’s #noprojects about 6 months after I started reading Allan Kelly’s LeanPub drafts of Continuous Digital. While Kelly’s Continuous Digital and his spin-off book <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/1912832046/ref=dbs_a_def_rwt_bibl_vppi_i2\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Project Myopia</a> focus on explaining the Why with some How topics, #noprojects has more of a team focussed view.</p>\r\n<p>It talks about the history of software development. It explains how we came to run software development with project structures and the inherent issues that came with them. It then outlines the case for continuous development with all the arguments for retaining knowledge, reducing handoff and dependences, etc.</p>\r\n<p>I think it is a great follow-on from Continuous Digital. Obviously, it is designed to be a stand-alone text but, for me, Kelly explains the Why of product development better. Then #noprojects fills in some additional background information and focusses more on the team level implementation. It might just be because I read Kelly’s book first but if you are looking to convince others in your organization about the need for transitioning to product development it is the go-to source. Reading both will provide a great foundation to understand and transition from projects to products.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4ae200c-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Project to Product\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4ae200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4ae200c-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Project to Product\" /></a>“<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Project-Product-Survive-Disruption-Framework/dp/B07KCLW84N/ref=sr_1_1?keywords=Project+to+Product%3A+How+to+Survive+and+Thrive+in+the+Age+of+Digital+Disruption+with+the+Flow+Framework&amp;qid=1560112109&amp;s=books&amp;sr=1-1\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Project to Product: How to Survive and Thrive in the Age of Digital Disruption with the Flow Framework</a></strong>”</p>\r\n<p><strong>Mik Kersten,  IT Revolution Press; November 2018</strong></p>\r\n<p>I first came across Mik’s work via a podcast he did with Shane Hastie. In the podcast, Mik explained that technologies often follow 50-year cycles and then transform through a tipping point into the next evolution in the way of working.</p>\r\n<p>Organizations that try to continue operating with the old model flounder and fail. Moreover, it is now about 50 years since NATO held the first conference on software engineering and the age of software began. Mik explained he believes we are at that tipping point and transitioning to product delivery will be part of the differentiator for the next wave of successful organizations.</p>\r\n<p>I have an interest in technology evolution stories and learning how ideas spread and then transform our lives. So, the 50-year cycle piqued my interest and I ordered the book. The book delivers and not only does it explain the tipping point we are living through right now, but it gives the best explanation of the digital revolution and need for digital transformation I have read to date. Kudos to Mik. If you want to explain why a digital transformation is necessary, and the implications of ignoring it, Project to Product is a brilliant source.</p>\r\n<p>After this great introduction, the remainder of the book explains the Flow Framework Mik helped develop and promotes through his company Tasktop Technologies. The Flow Framework provides metrics and tools for tracking and managing product development. This is useful because while the project management world has a wealth of information about tracking and managing projects, organizations that switch to product management often experience a void or competing recommendations.</p>\r\n<p>The Flow Framework is useful for explaining what we should pay most attention to tracking. Namely features, defects, risks and debts. It recommends a business outcome set of measures that include: value, cost, quality and happiness. The framework employs a lean inspired set of metrics that include flow velocity, flow efficiency, flow time, and flow load.</p>\r\n<p>It is in the rebranding of tradition lean metrics that I struggle to recommend the book wholeheartedly. By prefixing the normal throughput based lean metrics with the word “Flow” Mik is able to define  specific versions of the terms that are often implemented slightly differently from organization to organization. I can see the advantage of that, but it seems an unnecessary name-grab or overloading of lean terms to create trademark-able terms.</p>\r\n<p>That’s a minor quibble though and what Flow Framework does provide is a good mental model for organizing, executing and tracking your product development process. It nicely extends the pattern of books started with Continuous Digital that explains Why product development. Then #noprojects that provides some team-based and stewardship elements. Finally, Project to Product provides solid How To ideas for ongoing governance and improvement.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong>Summary</strong></p>\r\n<p>These three books form a useful progression for anyone wanting to learn about the product development trend. They each provide valuable ideas to help with understanding, practicing and then managing successful product delivery.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a48d1839200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Product Development Books Progression\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a48d1839200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a48d1839200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"Product Development Books Progression\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>The books allow readers to understand and internalize the need for a product development mindset. Then how to practice on a small scale before encouraging others to try and providing ways to measure and manage it.</p>\r\n<p>Obviously, you do not need to read all three of them, or in this sequence. Any one of them is a good read and source for practical ideas. However, I found that they build nicely upon each other and thought people would be interested to consider them as complementary.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Sun, 09 Jun 2019 20:32:49 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19fdc200b-350wi","linkMd5":"8f787bf08789d9555a3ba1c58efa1150","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn69@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-56-081_ae877ce6c7118cec.webp","destWidth":350,"destHeight":264,"sourceBytes":52995,"destBytes":12742,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19fdc200b-350wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn69@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-56-081_ae877ce6c7118cec.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b19f8a200b-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn83@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-50-05-356_0332616cb74eb0cd.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4a8200c-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn96@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-06-571_bd756197761970f9.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a463c4ae200c-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn72@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-50-05-585_030e923dec68303a.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a48d1839200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn44@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-50-08-173_946afc8b697be58f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Playing in the Gray of Hybrid","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2020/05/playing-in-the-gray-of-hybrid.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263ec16194f200c-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Playing in the Gray of Hybrid\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20263ec16194f200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263ec16194f200c-250wi\" style=\"width: 250px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Playing in the Gray of Hybrid\" /></a>Gray areas occupy the transition from one world to the next. Neither black nor white, predictive nor agile, project managers are increasingly finding themselves in the gray area of hybrid project management. This can make us feel uncomfortable since we are neither faithfully following either approach—instead living a compromise between seemingly different value systems.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">We could get uncomfortable, guarded and hesitant to embrace the reality we face. Or, we could welcome it, use it to our advantage and share the benefits/trade-offs with anyone willing to listen. This second option of embracing, using and sharing is “playing in the gray area,” a term I learned at a recent workshop I was giving. It nicely summarizes the idea of accepting and making the most of our reality rather than uncomfortably accommodating it and mainly keeping it to ourselves.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"><strong>Hybrid Environments</strong></span><br /><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">Let’s talk about what hybrid environments are and why we might find ourselves operating in one. A hybrid is a combination of two (or more) different systems. Hybrid cars can use internal combustion engines (ICE) and electric batteries, or ICE and hydrogen fuel cells. Both combinations are considered hybrids.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">In project environments, the use of both predictive approaches and agile approaches results in a hybrid approach. There are several reasons why both approaches might be used. Some common causes include:</span></p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"><em><strong> Agile is not entirely suitable </strong></em>– Agile approaches are applicable for high-change, uncertain, technically risky project work. They offer many great tools for engaging and empowering team members. They allow teams to go fast by streamlining communications and documentation. I am a strong advocate for using them where they work well. At the same time, I am a realist and understand they are not a panacea or silver bullet.</span></li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">Highly regulated industries require lots of documentation. Well-understood technology can be applied without the need for experiments and proof of concepts. Stable designs can be planned and executed with few change requests. Often, projects combine work that is well suited for predictive approaches and work that can benefit from agile approaches. Here it makes sense to use a hybrid approach overall.</span></p>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">We can execute the predictive work using predictive approaches and the adaptive work using agile approaches. For example, a project I worked on to develop and roll out a custom GPS routing solution for truck drivers used an agile approach for the software development—and a largely predictive approach for scheduling the equipment install in the trucks and training the drivers. We use the appropriate tools for the job at hand; it is neither rocket science or anything to be ashamed of.</span></p>\r\n<ol start=\"2\">\r\n<li><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"><em><strong> Operating in a predictive organization </strong></em>– Some organizations operate with upfront requirements analysis, scope sign-off and funding. We can try to educate stakeholders on why a more adaptive approach toward these operations might be beneficial, but maybe this is beyond our circle of influence—or maybe we inherited a project with a scope and budget already in place.</span></li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">Either way, sometimes we do not get to do everything by the book and we have to work with what we are given. This is not to say we should give up and not try to make improvements. Instead, accept that not everything will be ideal and that we need to choose our battles wisely. We might be asked/told to be the bridge between agile teams and not-so-agile organizational groups.</span></p>\r\n<ol start=\"3\">\r\n<li><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"><em><strong> Transitioning to agile incrementally </strong></em>– Large organizations rarely transition to agile approaches overnight. Some executives and training companies try a “sheep-dip” approach, immersing everyone in agile training and mandating a whole scale switch. However, these initiatives often fail. How big does the sheep-dip have to be? Does it include finance, HR and sales? How about procurement and suppliers? Usually, there are groups the team or supporting project managers still have to work with that have not transitioned to agile.</span></li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">Whenever an agile team works with a predictive entity, there is some mapping, interfacing and translating that needs to occur. This work often falls on the team lead or project manager (if one is in place). As depicted in Figure 1, these interfaces could be between our project and other projects (1), between an agile department and other non-agile departments (2) or from our department to the broader organization (3):</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9446240200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"Hybrid interfaces and buffering\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20263e9446240200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9446240200b-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Hybrid interfaces and buffering\" /></a></span></p>\r\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\"><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"><strong>Figure 1</strong>: Interactions that require hybrid interfaces and buffering</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">These scenarios are of course simplifications. Typically, organizational adoption of any idea, not just agile approaches, is fragmented and not uniform. Team leads and project managers often find themselves translating terminology, progress reports and plans to different stakeholders within the same meeting.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"><strong>Embrace and Own It</strong></span><br /><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">The flipside of uncertainty is being able to explain topics and help people learn. Anyone who can bridge between two worlds, two sets of concepts and two slightly different vocabularies is extremely valuable. Whether as a project manager, BA, product owner or executive, organizations benefit more from people willing and able to talk about the similarities, differences and problem areas than pure converted zealots. These interpreters and linkers can help other people make the transition.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">So, if asked “<em>When will your agile project be done?</em>”, rather than giving an eye roll for being asked such an uninformed question for an agile project—or mumbling, hand-waving or resorting to exotic terms about points, velocity and burn charts—instead, <em>own it</em>. This is an opportunity to talk about the gray area of hybrid metrics. So, a better response might be something along the lines of:</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\"><em>“Great question!, I ask this myself daily. Over the last four months</em><em>,</em><em> we have developed and gained business acceptance on about half of the features in the backlog (our total list of work), so that suggests we need another four months to finish. We have spent 45% of our budget, so we look on track for a Q4 completion within budget.”</em></span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">Likewise, actively combine predictive and agile components. Work with your team and product owner on getting your risk register responses correctly prioritized in the backlog. What are the opportunities we should exploit, and which threat avoidance and mitigation actions should we do first? With the shared goal of successful projects and happy stakeholders, there are many synergies to be found.</span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 10pt;\">The term “playing in the gray” has a couple of meanings. The first interpretation of “playing” addresses where we operate, our playing field (which these days is increasingly hybrid). The second meaning of playing is to enjoy things. Anyone bridging two environments has an opportunity to be useful, help people learn and add lots of value. It is a rewarding place to work, so own it and enjoy it.</span></p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[Note: For more articles from Mike Griffiths, visit his blog at <a href=\"http://www.leadinganswers.com/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">www.LeadingAnswers.com</a>. Mike first wrote this article for ProjectManagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/619824/Playing-in-the-Gray-of-Hybrid\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here]</a></span></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">Copyright © 2020 Mike Griffiths, Leading Answers Inc.</span></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Sat, 02 May 2020 00:43:01 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263ec16194f200c-250wi","linkMd5":"0c6dae3c3e0d594f864f686e99badd0d","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn56@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-57-200_3dec737aafb454a0.webp","destWidth":250,"destHeight":177,"sourceBytes":22386,"destBytes":3936,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263ec16194f200c-250wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn56@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-57-200_3dec737aafb454a0.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20263e9446240200b-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn64@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-874_16c7fcd588e055cd.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Webinar – Solving Wicked Problems: What is Old is New Again","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/07/webinar-solving-wicked-problems-what-is-old-is-new-again.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37e1479200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Problems\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37e1479200d image-full img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37e1479200d-800wi\" title=\"Problems\" /></a><br />My PMXPO webinar has now been watched by &gt; 11,000 people and received lots of positive feedback. They are the unique challenges never seen before in your organization, region or industry.</p>\r\n<p>As companies race to innovate and compete in a global market, we are seeing wicked problems more and more often. While the solution may be new, some common steps repeat in the stories of novel problem-solving successes through history. This presentation combines a fast-paced view of wicked problems and solutions through history—with a slower reveal of the common steps for solving challenging projects.</p>\r\n<p>It is ideal for anyone faced with managing projects with lots of uncertainty—or people looking to understand the links between lean, leadership, building collaborative teams and problem-solving.</p>\r\n<p><a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/videos/396963/PMXPO-2017--Solving-Wicked-Problems--What-is-Old-is-New-Again\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Watch Now</a>.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Tue, 03 Jul 2018 14:20:14 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37e1479200d-800wi","linkMd5":"28a0a2c33338b0acc438e7da8e1bfb67","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn63@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-56-919_644d5bfeb2d0db04.webp","destWidth":640,"destHeight":201,"sourceBytes":82765,"destBytes":40178,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad37e1479200d-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn63@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-56-919_644d5bfeb2d0db04.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"\"Agile Illustrated\" - Update","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/09/agile-illustrated-update.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b2c228200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); style=\"width: 350px;\" title=\"Encourage generalizing specialists\" /></a></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Fri, 27 Sep 2019 17:31:39 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b2c228200d-350wi","linkMd5":"174b9e37ffe7927e93e77276163afa51","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn7@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-55-962_2d8981a9383101eb.webp","destWidth":350,"destHeight":170,"sourceBytes":35929,"destBytes":9554,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b2c228200d-350wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn7@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-49-55-962_2d8981a9383101eb.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b2c25b200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn92@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-50-07-835_3b6164215a407c31.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4896ea8200c-350wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn88@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-50-07-868_22c999a03babeafd.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b2c290200d-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn36@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-342_df4ffa42adc03b3a.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4896ebb200c-350wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn59@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-50-08-198_b7d3b6e283bd0c27.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Organizational Structures that Support Faster Innovation and Evolution","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/12/organizational-structures-that-support-faster-innovation-and-evolution.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p>Organizational agility is the ability of an enterprise to change direction, realign and succeed in volatile, uncertain business environments. It requires sensing emerging trends and actively listening to customer requests, then acting on this information and making the changes required to position the organization for where it needs to be in the future.</p>\r\n<p>Small organizations can change direction quickly because they have fewer people or processes to change. Most medium to large-scale organizations have considerable mindset inertia in the form of strategies, multi-year plans, in-flight programs, and projects, etc. When fundamental change is required, it can be difficult to turn these large elements that have gathered their own momentum through the day-to-day behaviors of staff.</p>\r\n<p>Momentum is mass in motion. Think of a thousand people all moving toward a common goal—and their organizational structures and processes to get there. Now imagine the goal has shifted; we want to get to somewhere else. We need to shift all those minds - and likely much of the org structure and processes, this is a bigger ask requiring more energy.</p>\r\n<p><strong>The Efficiency vs. Adaptability Trade-off</strong><br />There is a growing trend. As rates of change increase, organizations are trading off efficiency for adaptability. Large-scale processes, specialized resources, and large batch sizes are optimized for maximum efficiency (the lowest cost per widget, the highest productivity rates per worker). From a cost-per-unit perspective, it’s hard to beat the scale, hierarchies, and specialization that are the lifeblood of efficient systems.</p>\r\n<p>Unfortunately, optimizing for efficiency decreases adaptability. A huge stamping press used for producing car door shells is great for fast, cheap output until someone wants a new car door design. Likewise, organizations created to optimize efficiency have a similar structure and process momentum toward a single fixed goal. This momentum is an obstacle if that goal moves or becomes a collection of separate moving goals.</p>\r\n<p><strong>The Diversity and Evolution of Organizations</strong><br />Fortunately, because there are no fixed ways of structuring a company (or operating one), we can learn from all the forms that have been tried and been successful—along with those that failed, too.</p>\r\n<p>Throughout history and alive today, we can find examples of organizations that exhibit varying degrees of structure/efficiency versus flexibility/adaptability. We have a rich gene pool of organizational diversity to study.</p>\r\n<p>Looking at organizations that do well in times of uncertainty can help us determine what qualities are required to thrive in high rates of uncertainty and complexity. Of course, we need to be careful with this natural selection approach, as good companies fail for a variety of reasons. But there are useful lessons and trends to observe.</p>\r\n<p><strong>An Autonomy Spectrum</strong><br />In the book <em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Reinventing-Organizations-Frederic-Laloux/dp/2960133501/ref=sr_1_3?keywords=Reinventing+Organizations&amp;qid=1569363026&amp;s=books&amp;sr=1-3\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Reinventing Organizations</a></em>, Frederic Laloux describes the evolution of organizations from primitive gangs ruled by fear, to sophisticated organizations that distribute power and decision making to local branches. The book is a fascinating read that assigns colors to each level of evolution and discusses many different attributes.</p>\r\n<p>However, for this article, I just want to focus on the structure/efficiency vs flexibility/adaptability element.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddb1200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Red Org\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddb1200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddb1200b-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px;\" title=\"Red Org\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #ff0000;\"><strong>Red Organizations</strong></span><strong><br /></strong>The most basic organizations impose pyramid-shaped command-and-control structures around the “Who?”, “What?” and “How?” aspects of work. Strict organizations like the Mafia or Catholic Church have a lot of structure around the <em>who</em>—only certain people get to make some decisions, and it is not questioned. Likewise, there is hierarchical control over <em>what</em> gets done and <em>how</em> it is done.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddc8200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Amber Org\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddc8200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddc8200b-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px;\" title=\"Amber Org\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #ffc800;\"><strong>Amber Organizations</strong></span><strong><br /></strong>The next level of evolution describes most traditional organizations. Here there is some flexibility on the <em>who</em>. Anyone should be able to rise to a position of power, but there is still a pyramid structure controlling <em>what</em> should be done—and standards and process for exactly <em>how</em> it should be achieved.</p>\r\n<p><em>Examples include government agencies and charter schools.</em></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddd5200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Orange Org\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddd5200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddd5200b-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px;\" title=\"Orange Org\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #ff8800;\"><strong>Orange Organizations</strong></span><strong><br /></strong>Orange organization make up the bulk of modern, profit-oriented enterprises. There is still a pyramid structure and control on <em>what</em> the company is focusing on, but freedom on <em>how</em> the results are obtained. Now people are empowered and encouraged to solve problems and achieve results.</p>\r\n<p><em>Examples include banks and retail organizations.</em></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce3402200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Green Org\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce3402200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce3402200d-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px;\" title=\"Green Org\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #00bf00;\"><strong>Green Organizations</strong></span><br />These are today’s modern organizations that encourage empowered teams. Within traditional pyramid structures, there is as much freedom as possible with <em>who</em>, <em>what</em> and <em>how</em> goals are accomplished. People are supported to own, define and improve how they work, so they improve not only the product, but the production process in which they work.</p>\r\n<p><em>Examples include Southwest Airlines, and W.L. Gore and Associates.</em></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2dddd200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Teal Org\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2dddd200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2dddd200b-200wi\" style=\"width: 200px;\" title=\"Teal Org\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"color: #00bfbf;\"><strong>Teal Organizations</strong></span><strong><br /></strong>Teal organizations break free from the pyramid structure and instead behave more like towns or organisms. Rather than try to funnel decision making and policy through a single hub, they spin-off and give authority to local groups. So local branches define new products, hire people, decide on pay rates, etc. Freed from a central, governing body, they are more autonomous and flexible.</p>\r\n<p><em>Examples include Buurtzorg Nursing and Morningstar.</em></p>\r\n<p>Laloux is careful to explain that this evolutionary progression is not necessarily from bad to good, or from worst to best. Instead, they just reflect different levels of consciousness and values within the organization. Also, different levels can exist within the same organization, nested like Russian dolls. For instance, a predominantly green organization can have an amber or red department within it where things are still very fixed with little flexibility.</p>\r\n<p><strong>From Control and Efficiency to Autonomy and Adaptation</strong></p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a50965200c-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Org Agility Spectrum\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a50965200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a50965200c-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"Org Agility Spectrum\" /></a><br /></strong></p>\r\n<p>The model is useful for showing the progression from a control and efficiency focus to one of autonomy and adaptation. In high-change environments, having a single hub for decision making slows the process down. Shoals of fish can change direction, split or merge to avoid prey in an instant. They do not take orders from a single source. Moving decision-making powers to those navigating improves responsiveness—but may lose out on efficiency and economies of scale.</p>\r\n<p>Organizations looking to improve their agility can examine where they are on the autonomy spectrum and determine what the next logical step for them would be if they want to be more adaptable (but potentially less efficient.) It is a trade-off; there is no single best organizational structure; it depends on the environment in which you operate.</p>\r\n<p>Some organizations spin-off start-ups to explore new product streams. These start-ups can be nimbler and operate unencumbered from the large pyramid structures and processes of the parent. Other organizations use Skunk Works groups to explore new ideas. Again, free from some of the normal scrutiny and controls that aim for efficiency, these insulated groups can innovate quicker.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Please Innovate While We Micromanage You!</strong><br />Organizations that demand innovation in highly controlled environments designed for process efficiency are likely in for a rude awakening. Organizations seem either structured for efficiency with strict controls on the <em>who</em>,<em> what </em>and<em> how</em>; or structured for flexibility, innovation, and adaptation.</p>\r\n<p>Much has changed since Laloux first published Reinventing Organizations in 2014 and many of the organizations he featured have gone on to evolve yet newer structures. However, the efficiency-adaptability spectrum remains a useful tool for understanding the type of organization we work in and for discussing the characteristics and challenges associated with organizational agility.</p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[This article first appeared on projectmanagement.com <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/579619/Organizational-Structures-that-Support-Faster-Innovation-and-Evolution\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Here</a>]</span></p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Wed, 04 Dec 2019 17:02:55 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddb1200b-200wi","linkMd5":"425bad51edc8a50a2f4e2cd1a3d15c6c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn72@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-720_7473a33d2cfb3d1d.webp","destWidth":200,"destHeight":193,"sourceBytes":34504,"destBytes":8866,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddb1200b-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn72@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-720_7473a33d2cfb3d1d.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddc8200b-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn35@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-50-06-338_547347f03eabbda2.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2ddd5200b-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn60@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-50-05-353_1d49d3553a44f76a.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce3402200d-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn23@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-50-05-625_67fce4a342b9e546.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2dddd200b-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn32@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-07-898_c2b0ca2eff9be6db.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a50965200c-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn20@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-50-08-388_511efb6f954f66b1.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995273},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"AI Assistants for Project Managers","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2018/07/ai-assistants-for-project-managers.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25edf200b-pi\"><img alt=\"Robot hand\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25edf200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25edf200b-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Robot hand\" /></a><br />Predictions like “AI will take our jobs” sound scary. However, long before our jobs as project managers are taken, AI will help us. In fact, it already is, and we don’t think about it much. While writing this article, AI in Microsoft Word and the add-in Grammarly helped protect you from the bulk of my spelling and grammar mistakes. This is how AI will help us first, by doing small things we are error-prone with, before tackling larger tasks.</p>\r\n<p>Like me, do you spend time booking meetings, finding rooms, and distributing information? Do you analyze backlogs and scope outlines for potential risks, or review estimates for commonly missed activities? Artificial Intelligence (AI) can help with these tasks and many more.</p>\r\n<p>Imagine having a non-judgemental expert monitoring everything you do (and do not do) at work and making helpful suggestions to you in private. This expert is constantly learning, is plugged into all the latest research and works for free. This is the not too distant future of AI assisted project management.</p>\r\n<p>June was Technology month at Project Management.com, and there have been a few articles about AI taking away project management jobs. This article focusses on ways AI can help project managers which will happen as AI develops and before it can replace jobs. It deals with automating the process and science parts of project management, leaving people more time to focus on the relationships, leadership, storytelling, empathy and emotional intelligence side of projects that are harder to tackle and are (currently) best done by people.</p>\r\n<p>AI has come a long way since Microsoft rolled out the annoying and not so helpful “Clippy” Office Assistant tool in 2003. It was never tuned for project managers, but it if were it might have looked something like this:</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25ecc200b-pi\"><img alt=\"Clippy\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25ecc200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25ecc200b-800wi\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"Clippy\" /></a>Instead, AI is becoming more sophisticated and useful. Gmail will remind you to attach a file if you mention “attach” in the text of an email that has no attachment. Most people use personal assistants like Siri and Cortana on their phones, or Alexa in their homes. Voice recognition and comprehension are steadily increasing. Google recently demonstrated their new Google Assistant calling and <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_ldoV5FQn0k&amp;feature=youtu.be&amp;t=43\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">interacting with a hair salon</a> to book a haircut. Clearly, these tools will soon be ready for prime time and their use will be widespread.</p>\r\n<p>Kevin Kelly, futurist and founding executive editor of Wired magazine, says in his TED talk: “<strong><span style=\"color: #0000bf;\"><em>Everything that we have electrified, we are now going to cognify</em></span></strong>”. In other words, we will add intelligence to devices and products. Kelly went on to say, “<em><strong><span style=\"color: #0000bf;\">I would suggest that the formula for the next 10,000 start-ups be very, very simple: take X - and add AI</span></strong>.</em>”</p>\r\n<p>To understand how AI can help project managers, let's examine its basic capabilities.</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Knowledge Based Expert System </strong><strong>(KBES)</strong> – these work from decision trees of IF - THEN statements to provide expertise. Gmail’s attachment reminder works with similar IF body_text includes “attach” AND Attachment = False THEN issue a warning.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Artificial Neural Network (ANN)</strong> – these systems model our real brains and consist of networks of weighted connections. They can be programmed to learn, recall, generalize and apply fuzzy logic. So, if we teach it someone 4ft high is Short and someone 6ft high is Tall it can generalize that someone 4ft 6 is “Not very tall”. Being able to make these types of generalizations are important for realistic interactions with people, such as Google Assistant making a hair appointment.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Machine Learning</strong> – this builds on Knowledge Based Expert Systems and Artificial Neural Networks to create predictive analytics that can provide validation and advice. In the project management space, this is the technology that can help with checking for missed risks, rebaselining plans, recalculating the Cost of Delay for waiting initiatives, etc.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Chatbots</strong> - AI powered programs designed to simulate a conversation with humans. Chatbots use artificial neural networks and machine learning to combine domain intelligence with natural language processing. This gives the impression of interacting with a (currently somewhat) knowledgeable person.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>If these technologies sound far-fetched in the project management field, consider the quote “<span style=\"color: #0000bf;\"><strong><em>The future is already here — it's just not very evenly distributed</em></strong></span>”. Agile tool vendor Atlassian, already provide project assistants that help with budgets, estimates, and sprint management. They also have chatbots to share project information and remind team members for estimates and status updates.</p>\r\n<p>Moving forward, these tools will be expanded to help check our work for common mistakes, just as Word checks for common spelling errors. Every industry has catalogs of defect origins and removal methods (<a href=\"http://www.ifpug.org/Documents/Jones-SoftwareDefectOriginsAndRemovalMethodsDraft5.pdf\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">here is one for software projects</a>) AI assistants can apply this knowledge and suggest steps to help avoid or reduce these risks. It is not an exact science and as a project manager, I may choose to dismiss potential risks flagged. However, having assistants available to highlight these risks or list the top 10 estimation omissions in my field is probably better than not having them.</p>\r\n<p>AI assistants can also alert project managers to slowly developing trends that might otherwise go unnoticed. The old saying that projects become late one day at a time is very true. Optimistic project managers with “Can-do” attitudes often underestimate the impact of small setbacks and or hope that teams will “catch-up” later.  This hardly ever happens, and AI assistants can be programmed to alert early and avoid hope-based-planning.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Over-Reliance?</strong></p>\r\n<p>There is a risk that with expert knowledge systems, organizations may be tempted to use inexperienced project managers. Or project managers become reliant upon these tools and not think as deeply as they may otherwise. Like any technology, a fool with a tool is still a fool. However, tapping into standard risk lists from your industry, that gets augmented with those from previous projects in your organization is a smart move.</p>\r\n<p>Having calculators has likely reduced our ability to perform long division calculations manually. However, I don’t want to go back to self-calculation just because I fear an over-reliance on technology. Instead, I want to use technology where I can and free up my time and mental capacity for other work.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Higher Value Work</strong></p>\r\n<p>The PMI Talent Triangle is a good model for thinking about all the work a project manager does. It includes: 1) Technical Project Management – the project mechanics described in the PMBOK Guide and Agile frameworks, 2) Strategic and Business Management – your industry-specific work, and 3) Leadership – the people dynamics of projects.</p>\r\n<p>If we squash the triangle out and lay the pieces in order of how much impact the project manager’s contribution has towards project success we get: Technical, then Strategic, and then Leadership. By this sequence, I mean that if the basics of Technical Management are met then Strategic and Business Management work is more significant. Furthermore, good Leadership has an even greater impact on overall project performance than Strategic and Business Management Work, and Technical Project Management.</p>\r\n<p>This sequence is shown below:</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"http://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25eac200b-pi\"><img alt=\"AI Focus\" border=\"0\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25eac200b image-full img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25eac200b-800wi\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"AI Focus\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>The good news for us as project managers is that (currently) AI is best suited for the lower value end of this work spectrum. It is already capable of assisting and saving us time with Technical Project Management work. Next, it should soon be commonplace to get AI assistance with Strategic and Business Management tasks. This will involve accessing machine learning focussed on our industry domains, like ROI models, common risks, and estimation omissions.</p>\r\n<p>The last area AI will move into is the Leadership domain. Machine learning requires deep data sets in a consistent form to draw reliable conclusions. The people dynamics of motivation, conflict management, and negotiation are harder to classify and rank.  Currently, most people would rather work with a real person to solve issues or discover their calling. Who knows, maybe in future people will prefer to interact with chatbots who’s decision parameters can be shown to be neutral and fair. This might be preferable to dealing with people with all their inherent bias and gaps in knowledge.</p>\r\n<p>All I know for now is that I currently welcome any AI assistance I can use. It is likely to safeguard me from making basic technical project management errors or omissions. It should also be helpful soon in providing industry knowledge and best practice – like having a seasoned professional in the industry available to look over your work. However, AI tools will check in real-time before you commit that decision or share a plan.</p>\r\n<p>This leaves me more time to focus on the people. The people sponsoring the project, those working on it, and those who will be impacted by it. They will have their own AI assistants too. Booking meetings, getting rooms, and sharing ideas should become frictionless leaving us to work on the more significant issues.</p>\r\n<p>My recommendation is to stay abreast of AI developments and remain open to trying the tool<span style=\"color: #0000bf;\">s<span style=\"color: #111111;\"> as they emerge.</span> <strong><em>Standing still in an environment that is moving forward has the effect of moving backwards</em></strong></span> -which is not good. Where I should probably be more worried is in writing articles like this. It seems like a blend of domain-specific Strategic work with some Leadership based storytelling. Likely a candidate for an AI takeover long before the project manager. (My plan is to get in on the research and get a Chatbot writing this stuff for as long as I can get away with it!)</p>\r\n<p><strong>References</strong>:</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.cio.com/article/3245773/project-management/how-ai-could-revolutionize-project-management.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">How AI could Revolutionize Project Management</a>, CIO Magazine, Mary Branscome, January 12 2018</li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.atlassian.com/blog/software-teams/3-ways-ai-will-change-project-management-better\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">3 ways AI will change project management for the better</a>, Atlassian Blog, April 7, 2017</li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.teodesk.com/blog/artificial-intelligence-in-project-management-is-your-company-ready-for-it/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Artificial Intelligence in Project Management - Is Your Company Ready for it?</a>, Teodesk Blog, Minja Belic, January 22 2018</li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://news.pwc.ch/wp-content/uploads/2018/04/AI-will-transform-PM-Whitepaper_EN_web.pdf\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">AI will Transform Project Management. Are You Ready?</a>, PWC White paper, Marc Lahmann, et Al, 2018</li>\r\n<li><a href=\"https://www.slideshare.net/AgileME/artificial-intelligence-in-project-management-by-dr-khaled-a-hamdy?qid=104f925b-25d2-4c78-86e2-22418696cb6a&amp;v=&amp;b=&amp;from_search=1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Artificial Intelligence in Project Management</a>, Khaled Hamdy, March 2017</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[Note: I wrote this article for ProjectManagement.com, it first appeared <a href=\"https://www.projectmanagement.com/articles/469866/AI-Assistants-for-Project-Managers\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">here </a>– free membership required.]</span></p>\r\n<p> </p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Fri, 20 Jul 2018 14:39:31 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25edf200b-600wi","linkMd5":"d5c4149e07d8330f511093203474afc6","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn84@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-49-55-730_8885a77ccf8e248a.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":232,"sourceBytes":92804,"destBytes":11384,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25edf200b-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn84@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-49-55-730_8885a77ccf8e248a.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25ecc200b-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn86@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-50-05-250_59b463c1a0933396.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2022ad3a25eac200b-800wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn96@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-50-08-610_04959022e51ae8ee.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995275},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"PMI-ACP Supported Self-Study Group with Mike Griffiths -
