This small group, seven-week program, is a cost-effective alternative to full-blown training courses for people able to work through my book independently.</p> \n <p>The program works as follows:</p> \n <ol> \n  <li>Read a chapter of my PMI-ACP Exam Prep book each week and work through the exercises</li> \n  <li>Join me for a 1-hour weekly Zoom call where we review the chapter and answer any questions</li> \n  <li>Access a private LinkedIn Group to discuss topics and exam preparation tips with peers</li> \n  <li>Gain access to exclusive domain mind-maps, extra sample exam questions and exam tips</li> \n </ol> \n <p>Benefits</p> \n <ul> \n  <li>Keep your studying on track by committing to a group with a schedule of one chapter a week</li> \n  <li>Much more cost-effective than in-person training (online or face-to-face)</li> \n  <li>Process the chapter during the week at your own pace, not the speed of a common group. (This <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2017/10/inverted-classrooms.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Inverted-Classroom</a> model allows for people to independently learn at their own pace and come together for discussions and questions.)</li> \n </ul> \n <p>New for this series</p> \n <ul> \n  <li>More sample exam questions</li> \n  <li>Exclusive study plan and exercise schedule</li> \n  <li>Additional LinkedIn group exercises and engagements</li> \n  <li>The first 25 people to signup will receive a free Kindle version of my book “<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Agile-Illustrated-Learners-Agility-Learning-ebook/dp/B07Y4DYGFF/ref=sr_1_1?crid=1HYDAFAZ76QEH&amp;dchild=1&amp;keywords=agile+illustrated+a+visual+learner%27s+guide+to+agility&amp;qid=1597203186&amp;sprefix=agile+ill%2Caps%2C194&amp;sr=8-1\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>Agile Illustrated: A Visual Learners Guide to Agility</strong></a>” that explains each of the PMI-ACP exam content outline domains and tasks.</li> \n </ul> \n <p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2026be407cf9c200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\"><img alt=\"PMI-ACP Supported Self-Study Group with Mike Griffiths\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e2026be407cf9c200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2026be407cf9c200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;\" title=\"PMI-ACP Supported Self-Study Group with Mike Griffiths\" /></a></p> \n <p>If you have considered getting your PMI-ACP or need a boost committing to an exam date, this is a great opportunity.</p> \n <p>Email <a href=\"mailto:SeptemberACP@gmail.com\">SeptemberACP@gmail.com</a> for more details.</p>\n</div>","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Wed, 12 Aug 2020 03:42:22 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2026be407cf7c200d-600wi","linkMd5":"6610203d3a8cbeb1bb1cf27ce70363bc","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn36@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-50-03-312_960243618ec1d5d5.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":338,"sourceBytes":4444881,"destBytes":3463944,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2026be407cf7c200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn36@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-50-03-312_960243618ec1d5d5.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e2026be407cf9c200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn52@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-50-06-800_396fc4d9218aa0f1.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995272},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Agile 2019 Presentations","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/03/agile-2019-presentations.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a73d9200b-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"DC Conference\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a73d9200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a73d9200b-300wi\" style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"DC Conference\" /></a>I learned this week that two of my presentation submissions for the Agile 2019 conference in Washington D.C. August 6-10 have been accepted. I was very lucky to get two accepted as they received nearly 2,000 submissions for around 250 slots. It should be fun and I am really looking forward to it.</p>\r\n<p>My talks will be on moving beyond agile approaches and case studies in transitioning from projects to products.  Here are the outlines:</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong>The Future Looks Awesome, and Moving Beyond Agile - </strong>The Future of Agile Software Development (IEEE Software) track. <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a735d200b-pi\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"The Future is Awesome\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a735d200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a735d200b-250wi\" style=\"width: 250px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"The Future is Awesome\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><em>“Agile approaches succeeded and changed the way we work. They brought the philosophy and tools previously used by only the high performing teams to the majority of organizations. Now it is time to move beyond them and embrace a new wave of emerging ideas and approaches.</em></p>\r\n<p><em>It is short-sighted and self-absorbed to imagine agile approaches represent the best way to execute all work types. As new technology, products, and services emerge, we need new ways to deliver them. Likewise, as organizational structures evolve to use this technology and integrate the aspirations of next-generation workers - who grew up in a digital world, our approaches much evolve again.</em></p>\r\n<p><em>Fortunately, patterns are emerging from new organizational structures and the lessons from failed agile transformations. Agile’s “Family” mindset of empowerment and values-driven culture is being overtaken by “Organism” and “Community” mindset organizations embracing Holacracy and Teal Organization ideas. People are also realizing not everyone wants to adopt an agile mindset and we need better ways of integrating with more traditional models that remain that way for their own advantages. The future involves further expansion and integration, not more fervent conceptual conversion.</em></p>\r\n<p><em>Come and examine the future beyond agile and hybrid agile. Explore the trends in corporate structures, career aspirations, engagement models, and the technology that is making it all possible. The future is exciting, dynamic, and decidedly less agile – but in a good way.”</em></p>\r\n<p>It will be a fast-moving presentation recapping the rise, role, and results of agile approaches before moving onto emerging trends. Through case studies of successful organizations, we will explore new patterns in work, the worker, and the workplace.</p>\r\n<p>Agile approaches play an integral role and will continue to in the future. However, they are already being augmented and extended by additional models and techniques. Using a “Yes, and…” mindset of combining approaches we examine emerging trends and what they may mean for the future of collaborative work.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a44ca439200c-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Pothole\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a44ca439200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a44ca439200c-200wi\" style=\"width: 175px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Pothole\" /></a>Speed-bumps and Potholes on the Road from Projects to Products - </strong>Experience Reports track.</p>\r\n<p><em>“Transitioning from projects to products made perfect sense for my client. Much of the business was digital and their websites / online-services would not be “completing” or going away soon. Development was deliberately continuous, and executives embraced this as both inevitable and desirable. However, just because it was the logical thing to do, it did not mean it was easy.</em></p>\r\n<p><em>Maybe if we did not have over 100 inflight projects executing simultaneously, we could have picked an easier starting time? Maybe if there were not so many dependencies between teams, work would have been easier to untangle? Maybe if they were not in the midst of transitioning to microservices and new hosting technology, the technology challenges would have been easier to resolve?</em></p>\r\n<p><em>Most organizations considering the transition from projects to products have similar challenges. By definition, “transitioning” means doing things mid-process; otherwise, it would be “starting fresh with product development” – and where’s the fun in that?</em></p>\r\n<p><em>This experience report recounts the story and transformation from slick PowerPoint slides to people problems and development difficulties. We did survive the journey and arrived in the land of continuous digital delivery, but we took some detours and lost some paint along the way. If you are considering the switch from projects to product development, maybe we can help you avoid some potholes and speedbumps along the road. Being forewarned is to be forearmed, but each journey is different, and as they say, your mileage will vary.”</em></p>\r\n<p>It recounts my experience as a consultant working with the leadership team and development teams. There was general agreement that a shift to organizing around products made sense, but disagreement on the best way to get there. Rather than a big-bang changeover, we adopted a ripple-out, incremental approach. This allowed us to review and monitor for issues before spreading approaches to more teams. Naturally, the executives wanted everything done at once and the teams wanted to be left alone until after the next release.</p>\r\n<p>Not being able to please everyone, we developed a workable plan and rolled out the changes to teams, technology, and supporting groups. We experienced many obstacles such as having to rewrite vendor contracts, get exceptions from accounting for budget processes and from HR for reporting changes.</p>\r\n<p>More details about the Agile 2019 conference in Washington D.C. can be found on the <a href=\"https://www.agilealliance.org/agile2019/\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">official website</a>.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Sat, 30 Mar 2019 02:07:22 +0000","feedId":8088,"bgimg":"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a73d9200b-300wi","linkMd5":"e2bfda83bcf7f4842a987987ff3eb783","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn98@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-752_b8c007ab69c64774.webp","destWidth":300,"destHeight":162,"sourceBytes":67240,"destBytes":9364,"author":"Mike Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a73d9200b-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn98@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-49-55-752_b8c007ab69c64774.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a49a735d200b-250wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn12@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-50-07-803_6b305c6ee8ef9d90.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a44ca439200c-200wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn16@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-50-07-047_f4936a4fdd14d468.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Innovation: Running Experiments and Learning","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/06/innovation-running-experiments-and-learning.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6728d200b-pi\" style=\"float: right;\"><img alt=\"Experiment Design\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6728d200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6728d200b-300wi\" style=\"width: 300px; margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;\" title=\"Experiment Design\" /></a>In my last article on <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/06/incubating-innovation.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Incubating Innovation</a>, we explored the culture and mindset of accountable experimentation. This article focuses on actionable tools and approaches.</p>\r\n<p>Within agile frameworks, the team retrospective is the primary workshop for planning and evaluating experiments. Yet most team retrospectives I see are broken.</p>\r\n<p>Teams spend too much time recording viewpoints and information—but not enough time reviewing or planning experiments. It is common to see the majority of the time spent gathering what went well, what did not go well, and appreciations. Yet where’s the focus on experiments, the learning process and trials for the next iteration?</p>\r\n<p>To make things worse, some teams do not take the retrospective seriously. Maybe after the potential stress of the sprint review, the largely internal retrospective is a relief. A chance to chill out, maybe share some food, and pat each other on the back. However, innovation and learning take conscious effort, forward planning and accountability.</p>\r\n<p>As I work with organizations, I often sit in on retrospectives. Of all the regular workshops/ceremonies, these sessions are typically the least prepared for and worst executed. I often see lazy retrospectives where a basic lessons-learned format is used, but timings are not managed and the recommendations for the next sprint get skimped as they run out of time.</p>\r\n<p>The pie chart below shows a typical planned allocation of time—and the reality of how time is actually spent:</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4689a13200c-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"R1\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4689a13200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4689a13200c-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"R1\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>In these lazy retrospectives, people are slow to start, spend longer on recording what went well than what could be improved, and then try to cram the recommendations for experiments (the most important part) into the last few minutes. As a result, experimentation suffers. Few experiments are scheduled for the next sprint, and those that are run are not evaluated properly.</p>\r\n<p>This is not how agile retrospectives are supposed to operate. An excellent guide to running effective retrospectives is <em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Agile-Retrospectives-Making-Pragmatic-Programmers-ebook/dp/B00B03SRJW/ref=sr_1_fkmrnull_1?crid=1AX3M4NO4FK4U&amp;keywords=agile+retrospectives+making+good+teams+great&amp;qid=1550716925&amp;s=gateway&amp;sprefix=agile+retrospe%2Caps%2C193&amp;sr=8-1-fkmrnull\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Agile Retrospectives: Making Good Teams Great</a></em> by Esther Derby and Diana Larsen. In it they describe a five-step process:</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li><strong>Set the stage</strong> – Help people focus on the task at hand; check that people are ready and willing to contribute. Outline and gain consensus on the process we will use. <em>Techniques we can use include: check-in, working agreements, focus-on/focus-off (see the book for full descriptions of these techniques).</em></li>\r\n<li><strong>Gather data</strong> – Create a shared view of what happened during the sprint/iteration. When completed, we should have a common understanding of the observations and facts. <em>Team activities we can use include: timeline, mad-sad-glad, team radar.</em></li>\r\n<li><strong>Generate insights</strong> – This focuses on understanding the implications of our findings and discussions. We need to see the impacts of the problems we are faced with before trying to solve them. <em>Techniques we can use include: five whys, fishbone analysis, dot voting.</em></li>\r\n<li><strong>Decide what to do</strong> – Now we move from thinking about the iteration that just ended to what we should try next to improve things. We identify the highest-priority action items, create plans for experiments and set measurable goals to achieve the desired results. <em>Techniques we can use include: SMART goals, circle of questions, short subjects.</em></li>\r\n<li><strong>Close the retrospective</strong> – Here we reflect on the retrospective process and express our appreciations. We may summarize what we have decided to keep or change and what we are thankful for. <em>Team-based activities we can use include: plus/delta, return of time invested (ROTI), appreciations.</em></li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>This is a more useful format. However, despite people having access to good retrospective advice, poor implementations are still common. Teams continue to attend late, start slowly and run out of time or rush the agreement on what experiments to run.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6781e200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"R2\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6781e200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6781e200b-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"R2\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>The recurring theme is poor experimentation design and restricted learning. Gunther Verheyen summarized the problem nicely in his recent post entitled “<a href=\"https://dzone.com/articles/inspection-without-adaptation-is-pointless-in-scru?utm_medium=feed&amp;utm_source=feedpress.me&amp;utm_campaign=Feed:%20dzone%2Fagile\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Inspection Without Adaptation Is Pointless</a>.” Gathering data and deciding what to do is pointless if it is not acted upon. We are doing most of the preparation work but not getting any of the benefits.</p>\r\n<p><strong>Experiment Design to the Rescue</strong><br />Fortunately, there are some good models we can use. We need to manage time and effort more effectively and use retrospectives to plan and evaluate more experiments. We should spend only 50% of the available time on gathering information and the remainder reviewing the results of past experiments, making wins part of our process, designing new experiments and learning from inevitable failures.</p>\r\n<p>We can help the time management problem by assigning work to be done in advance. People should be thinking about issues and potential solutions independently. There are benefits of group discussion and consensus gathering on agreed experiment design, but observation and idea generation is best done individually.</p>\r\n<p><em>The New Yorker </em>magazine [3] describes numerous studies that show how brainstorming groups think of fewer, lower-quality ideas than the same number of people who work alone and later pool their ideas. There have been numerous reports on the downsides of brainstorming ideas as a group. Groupthink and the halo effect stifle idea generation. So, ask for people to come with ideas, then use the group setting to vet and vote for them.</p>\r\n<p>Visualizing the ideas and experiments is an effective way to bring collective attention to them. Trent Hone and Andrew Jarding developed the “Ideas and Experiments board” pictured below. It shows the progression of ideas through experiments and their success or abandonment:</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b67486200b-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Experiment Board\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b67486200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b67486200b-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"Experiment Board\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\"><strong>Ideas and Experiments Board (Image Credit: Trent Hone and Andrew Jarding, MindSettlers)</strong></span></p>\r\n<p>As discussed in the <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/06/incubating-innovation.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">last article</a>, by design, 50% of our experiments should fail since we are trying to maximize our learning, not validate things we already know. So I would expect to see an equal number of abandoned experiments as successful ones.</p>\r\n<p>However, this format (or a slightly modified version that represents an experiments Kanban board) is a useful tool to bring the focus for retrospectives to the experiments being run and considered. With some pre-work on idea generation and an increased focus on experiments, we can structure more effective retrospectives.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0e9200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"R3\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0e9200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0e9200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"R3\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>This retrospective format saves some time by assigning idea generation as pre-work; this also helps avoid the groupthink pitfalls. It furthermore places emphasis on the experiments—the inputs for learning and innovation.</p>\r\n<p>I have experienced pushback from teams about the goal of 50% experiment failure. People understand it optimizes learning—but say it sets people up for too much failure. I understand the sentiment but counter with two perspectives.</p>\r\n<p>First, these are experiments; they should be dispassionate explorations, not evaluations of the people undertaking the work. We need to be professional and try to overcome habits of internalizing results. I know this is easier said than done, so also offer a second reason: We need to kill bad ideas early to save time and money for better ones.</p>\r\n<p>In the article “<a href=\"https://hbr.org/2019/01/the-hard-truth-about-innovative-cultures\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">The Hard Truth About Innovative Cultures</a>,” Gary Pisano describes how killing bad ideas is critical. He profiles Flagship Pioneering, a Massachusetts-based R&amp;D company. It uses a disciplined exploration approach to run small experiments minimizing expenditure. Instead of running experiments to validate ideas, it designs “killer experiments” to maximize the probability of exposing an idea’s flaws. The goal is to learn what went wrong early and move in a more promising direction.</p>\r\n<p>Other useful ideas from the paper include:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n<li><strong>Tolerance for failure, but no tolerance for incompetence</strong> – Hire the best people you can. Explain the goals clearly and let go of those that do not perform.</li>\r\n<li><strong>Psychologically safe, but brutally candid </strong>– Encourage frank but respectful two-way dialog. It may feel uncomfortable, but it can prevent issues or concerns from going unreported.</li>\r\n<li><strong>Collaboration—but with individual accountability </strong>– Encourage discussions, but avoid groupthink and hold people accountable for decisions and outcomes.</li>\r\n</ul>\r\n<p>These are all great concepts and align with the frustrations I experience when I see teams not taking retrospectives seriously—or following through on conducting experiments. I realized I needed a better model for discussing the problem. Fortunately, I found the field of collaborative problem solving (CPS).</p>\r\n<p>CPS is the study of how we work together in groups to solve new problems, innovate and build products. The innovation process and retrospective workshop fall squarely within its scope. CPS skills are quite separate from individual task-focused skills, meaning people can be great at working individually but poor at working together.</p>\r\n<p>A good introduction to CPS frameworks can be found in the article “<a href=\"https://journals.sagepub.com/stoken/default+domain/10.1177%2F1529100618808244-free/full\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Advancing the Science of Collaborative Problem Solving</a>.” One model they feature is the “PISA 2015 Collaborative Problem-Solving Assessment.” Unfortunately, like many academic models, the degree of difficulty goes downward, which may make sense as you read down through more advanced stages. However, I think graphically, so I have redrawn the model to show degrees of completeness and difficulty radiating up and out from a 0,0 origin, as shown below:</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0fa200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"PISA 1\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0fa200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0fa200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"PISA 1\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>Along the X-axis, we see three categories of collaborative problem-solving competencies. These are:</p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li>Establishing a shared understanding</li>\r\n<li>Taking action to solve the problem</li>\r\n<li>Establishing and maintaining team organization</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>Up the Y-axis, we have four categories of problem-solving:</p>\r\n<ol style=\"list-style-type: upper-alpha;\">\r\n<li>Understanding the problem</li>\r\n<li>Representing the problem</li>\r\n<li>Planning and executing</li>\r\n<li>Monitoring and reflecting</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p>Within the body of the model, each square is labeled with the column number and row letter, and describes the tasks that occur in that space.</p>\r\n<p>The model provides a diagnostic tool for identifying broken and lazy retrospectives. The poor engagement and weak follow-through I see in many Scrum teams is characterized by an incomplete execution of column 1 and only half-completion of columns 2 and 3 (as shown by the red outline below):</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d105200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"PISA 2\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d105200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d105200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"PISA 2\" /></a></p>\r\n<p>Teams are not spending time in “(D1) – Monitor and repairing the shared understanding,” nor are they getting to the “(C2) Enacting plans,” (D2), (D3) and (C3) areas to follow through on plans and hold each other accountable for actions and results.</p>\r\n<p>What we want is a complete execution of all the collaborative problem-solving competencies; only then is the framework complete (along with the feedback mechanisms to keep things in check and moving in the right direction):</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d10f200d-popup\" onclick=\"window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"PISA 3\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d10f200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d10f200d-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"PISA 3\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Summary</strong><br />Innovation involves combining the right mindset and philosophy with tools and practical steps to ensure its execution. Motivation and attitude are paramount; people have got to <em>want</em> to do this work, enjoy it and create a pull demand for the tools and process that enable it. Trying to foster innovation with demotivated teams is like trying to push a rope.</p>\r\n<p>When motivated and happy people create a strong pull demand for innovation, we need to be ready with the right tools to support the process and keep the momentum going. This includes designing experiments to maximize learning and killing bad ideas quickly—all while demanding competence, accountability and candor.</p>\r\n<p>It is not easy to master the combination of soft skills and techniques required for successful improvement and innovation. However, organizations that succeed can respond to market changes faster and are poised to exploit new technologies and opportunities. Ideas and inventions are spreading quicker than ever. Learning how to build collaborative, innovative teams has become a critical skill.</p>\r\n<p><strong><em>References</em></strong></p>\r\n<ol>\r\n<li>Book: <em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Agile-Retrospectives-Making-Pragmatic-Programmers-ebook/dp/B00B03SRJW/ref=sr_1_fkmrnull_1?crid=1AX3M4NO4FK4U&amp;keywords=agile+retrospectives+making+good+teams+great&amp;qid=1550716925&amp;s=gateway&amp;sprefix=agile+retrospe%2Caps%2C193&amp;sr=8-1-fkmrnull\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Agile Retrospectives: Making Good Teams Great</a></em> by Esther Derby and Diana Larsen</li>\r\n<li>Article and video: “<a href=\"https://dzone.com/articles/inspection-without-adaptation-is-pointless-in-scru?utm_medium=feed&amp;utm_source=feedpress.me&amp;utm_campaign=Feed:%20dzone%2Fagile\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Inspection Without Adaptation Is Pointless</a>” by Gunther Verheyen</li>\r\n<li>Article: “<a href=\"http://www.newyorker.com/reporting/2012/01/30/120130fa_fact_lehrer\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Groupthink: the Brainstorming Myth</a>” by Jonah Lehrer</li>\r\n<li>Article: “<a href=\"https://hbr.org/2019/01/the-hard-truth-about-innovative-cultures\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">The Hard Truth About Innovative Cultures</a>” by Gary Pisano</li>\r\n<li>Article: “<a Arthur Graesser, et al.</li>\r\n</ol>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 8pt;\">[Note: I first wrote this article for projectmanagement.com <a Griffiths","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6728d200b-300wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn60@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-49-57-300_949f1e78598c52b0.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4689a13200c-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn20@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-50-06-791_f4611d82f5255820.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b6781e200b-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn100@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-384_815502c3fc5f0ba9.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4b67486200b-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn35@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-50-07-598_781847b45c422e06.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0e9200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn64@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-50-06-727_6d9ba2ed3287e9d8.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d0fa200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn60@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-50-07-521_2ae26ab68bd326ce.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d105200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn80@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-50-05-477_f84f9396877833be.webp","https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a491d10f200d-600wi":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn8@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-50-05-497_2a00bc30f0dee812.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598312995274},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:49:55","title":"Agile Illustrated – Sample #3","link":"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/12/agile-illustrated-sample-3.html","description":"\r\n<div xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml\"><p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a5201b200c-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Agile Illustrated - Cover small\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a5201b200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a5201b200c-250wi\" style=\"width: 250px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Agile Illustrated - Cover small\" /></a>This is the third sample from my new Kindle book “<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B07Y4DYGFF?pf_rd_p=2d1ab404-3b11-4c97-b3db-48081e145e35&amp;pf_rd_r=FJVT5B3GZQ9B4NMHWN62\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Agile Illustrated: A Visual Learner’s Guide to Agility</a>”. The book is a graphical introduction to the agile mindset and servant leadership behaviors for working with agile teams. If you missed the first two samples you can find them <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/10/agile-illustrated-sample-1.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a> and <a href=\"https://www.leadinganswers.com/2019/11/agile-illustrated-sample-2.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>.</p>\r\n<p>Also, just in time for Christmas, Agile Illustrated is now available as a physical paperback book. So if you prefer to hold a physical book rather than read a Kindle book you can now get your hands on a copy. Or, if you would like to give a copy to a manager or executive who is unlikely to read a normal length book on the agile mindset and how to support agile teams then buy them a copy as a gift.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a522ab200c-pi\" style=\"float: left;\"><img alt=\"Agile Illustrated New Physical Book\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a522ab200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a522ab200c-100wi\" style=\"width: 100px; margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;\" title=\"Agile Illustrated New Physical Book\" /></a>At just 88 pages and mainly pictures it is a quick read that explains the agile values, principles and servant leadership behaviors needed to support agile teams. Available from your local Amazon online store, the US link is <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Agile-Illustrated-Learners-Agility-Learning/dp/1695681231/ref=tmm_pap_swatch_0?_encoding=UTF8&amp;qid=1575494304&amp;sr=8-1\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>.</p>\r\n<p>Today we will review Team Performance. The Team Performance domain includes Team Formation, Team Empowerment, and Team Collaboration activities. (Anyone taking the PMI-ACP exam should expect to see 18-20 questions on this topic.)</p>\r\n<p>Here is a mindmap showing all the tasks, we will then review them one at a time.</p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a52057200c-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"Domain_04_d (1)\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a52057200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a52057200c-600wi\" style=\"width: 600px;\" title=\"Domain_04_d (1)\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 12pt;\"> <strong>Team Formation</strong></span></p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a00200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D41\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a00200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a00200d-250wi\" style=\"width: 217px;\" title=\"D41\" /></a><br /> </strong><strong>Task 1 – Jointly create team norms</strong></p>\r\n<p>Learn how people want to work and agree on how things should be done and how issues should be handled.</p>\r\n<p>As a group, develop the group rules that will be followed. By being involved in the creation of the team norms, people are much more likely to feel ownership and commitments towards them. Telling people how we should work is much less empowering than engaging those people in jointly developing their own framework.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a0c200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D42\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a0c200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a0c200d-250wi\" style=\"width: 221px;\" title=\"D42\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Task 2 – Help develop technical and interpersonal skills</strong></p>\r\n<p>Encourage the development of technical and people skills so everyone is equipped to work effectively.</p>\r\n<p>Knowledge work requires two sets of skills. The first is to do the technical work as a subject matter expert (SME), the second is to work productively with other SMEs and stakeholders, including the business and customer. The job of learning and honing these skills is never done, and we should always be improving our technical and collaboration skills.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 12pt;\"><strong>Team Empowerment</strong></span></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a52079200c-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D43\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a52079200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a52079200c-300wi\" style=\"width: 253px;\" title=\"D43\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Task 3 – Encourage generalizing specialists</strong></p>\r\n<p>Encourage people to have a broad range of skills, not only deep, narrow ones, so that as workload varies people can help other team members out.</p>\r\n<p>The concept of “T” shaped people rather than “I” shaped people captures the idea of having skills in surrounding fields of work, in addition to a specialization. To maximize the value delivered we want global rather than local optimization. This means focusing on overall throughput of value over people-utilization efficiencies. T-shaped people are valuable for optimizing value since they allow us to share work to reduce bottlenecks.</p>\r\n<p><strong> </strong></p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a28200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D44\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a28200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a28200d-200wi\" style=\"width: 180px;\" title=\"D44\" /></a><br /> </strong><strong>Task 4 – Empower team members</strong></p>\r\n<p>Encourage people to step up for new roles. Allow them to make their own decisions. Put them in charge of many elements of their job.</p>\r\n<p>We want people to take ownership of their work and start to make their own improvements. So encourage people to look for opportunities for improvements and take initiative to make them happen. These are forms of emergent and shared leadership. Subject matter experts know their domains best, so empower them to manage complexity and create solutions to the problems they face.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3da200b-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D45\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3da200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3da200b-250wi\" style=\"width: 233px;\" title=\"D45\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Task 5 – Proactively manage morale</strong></p>\r\n<p>Learn what motivates people and provide that motivation in their workplace.</p>\r\n<p>Frequently observe and ask team members about what motivates them individually and as part of a team. Also, learn what demotivates or upsets them. Then try to find ways to improve the work environment to foster happiness, productivity, and satisfaction.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 12pt;\"><strong>Team Collaboration and Commitment</strong></span></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3e7200b-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D46\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3e7200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3e7200b-300wi\" style=\"width: 283px;\" title=\"D46\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Task 6 – Encourage ongoing communications</strong></p>\r\n<p>Encourage dialog and technology that helps share information.</p>\r\n<p>Usually, the best way to help communications is to physically co-locate with the people you need to communicate with. Nothing beats seeing them and talking with them. It allows for the richest exchange of information accompanied by body language and emotion.</p>\r\n<p>When colocation is not possible, provide the best tools you can to keep people in communication. Monitor communications and look for ways to reduce miscommunication or address missing communication. This helps reduce costly and wasteful rework caused by miscommunication.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a520db200c-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D47\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a520db200c img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4a520db200c-320wi\" style=\"width: 319px;\" title=\"D47\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Task 7 – Protect team from distractions</strong></p>\r\n<p>Shield the team from interruptions.</p>\r\n<p>Distractions and low-priority interruptions can come from many sources. They might be requests from superfluous sources or demands for low-priority admin work. Even quick interruptions cause task-switching and interrupting flow.</p>\r\n<p>Special-ops and Skunkworks teams have been effective and highly productive in part because they were separated and shielded from interruptions.</p>\r\n<p> </p>\r\n<p><strong> <a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3f1200b-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D48\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3f1200b img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4f2f3f1200b-300wi\" style=\"width: 265px;\" title=\"D48\" /></a><br /></strong><strong>Task 8 – (Re)communicate the vision to align the goal</strong></p>\r\n<p>Show the end goal and how people’s contributions help get us there.</p>\r\n<p>People should understand how their work contributes to the end goal. So we need to align the team goals with the product or project goal and show the connections and steps along the way to our final destination.</p>\r\n<p><strong> </strong></p>\r\n<p><a class=\"asset-img-link\" href=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a4d200d-pi\" style=\"display: inline;\"><img alt=\"D49\" class=\"asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a4d200d img-responsive\" src=\"https://leadinganswers.typepad.com/.a/6a00d834527c1469e20240a4ce4a4d200d-250wi\" style=\"width: 219px;\" title=\"D49\" /></a></p>\r\n<p><strong>Task 9 – Measure performance to help forecasting</strong></p>\r\n<p>Encourage people to measure and share their performance so we can get better at forecasting at a high level.</p>\r\n<p>In order to improve our ability to forecast, we need to track how things actually turned out. If we keep making estimates without checking actual performance, we will keep making the same estimation errors. Tracking velocity and work delivered helps create a more accurate view of the team’s true capacity for future work.</p>\r\n<p>I hope you enjoyed these samples from my most recent book. It was a fun project for me and my wish is that people find it an easy introduction to agile values.</p></div>\r\n","descriptionType":"xhtml","publishedDate":"Mon, 09 Dec 2019 14:21:00
